Encore (A Special Report): Essay --- When a Child Returns Home: At times, freedom and privacy can't hold a candle to the fullness of family...
Encore (A Special Report): Essay --- When a Child Returns Home: At times, freedom and privacy can't
hold a candle to the fullness of family life
By Ellen Graham
1,434 words
26 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
R12
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE LITTLE DETAILS of that afternoon have always remained with me. The slanting rays of a setting winter
sun glazed our Connecticut kitchen with a cozy, golden sheen. Our daughter Julia, then 17, slung her
backpack on her shoulders, gave me a hurried kiss goodbye and hopped in the car to drive back to her
friends -- her real life -- in boarding school a couple of hours away. Christmas break was over, and for the first
time it hit me: Never again would she be anything but a temporary visitor in our home.
That stark realization caused me plenty of tears, and I moped for days. Here's what I've learned, however:
Never say never.
Our nest, so wrenchingly empty that evening 10 years ago, is bursting at the seams again. Julia, having
finished college and worked for some years in Key West, Fla., and Denver, returned home this spring, along
with her new husband, their 60-pound Doberman puppy and a fluffy tabby cat. Home is no longer the
Connecticut suburbs; we retired five years ago to a small college town in Virginia, a place Julia had always
seemed to dismiss as hopelessly provincial.
We offered a safe port in a stormy economy that has battered many a 20-something. The toxic mix of college
and credit-card debt, stratospheric rents and uncertain career prospects had left Julia scrambling from
paycheck to paycheck. Her husband, Silver, a native of Estonia, needed to get working papers and a Social
Security card. By moving into our guest room and paying us a modest rent, they figured they could regain
their footing.
This homecoming was completely out of character for Julia, whose fierce independence had defined her very
being. One of her first full sentences -- chirped as I spoon-fed her in her high chair -- was, "I do it self!" Asking
for help has never come easily to her, and her misgivings were all too apparent when she turned up on our
doorstep.
I, in turn, felt defensive when friends' eyebrows shot up and they quizzed us about how long our new
boarders would be staying. The unspoken implication: Kiss your freedom and privacy goodbye. Not that our
arrangement was that unusual: Among just our own friends and neighbors, I can count eight retired couples
now sharing their homes with adult children.
Admittedly, we were all on edge at the outset. Adjusting has called for my learning a whole new set of dance
steps, so to speak. I shrug off the trivial stuff, the general air of disruption that comes from having 12 extra
legs in the house: the dishwasher and laundry room humming 24/7; the piles of sneakers in the entry hall; the
light dusting of tiny needlelike Doberman hairs that drift over every surface and can lodge in your eye or toe
like splinters.
My husband and I even showed admirable restraint when the puppy unexpectedly came into heat, was
outfitted in a pair of denim diapers and proceeded to drive our geriatric mini-dachshund, Tucker, into
paroxysms of unrequited desire.
More difficult has been exposing ourselves to a running commentary on our habits, beliefs and
idiosyncrasies. "Why do you guzzle gas with daily trips to the grocery?" Julia will ask. (To restock the
refrigerator, which lately empties with astonishing speed.) Our boisterous political discussions don't go down
well, especially before breakfast. Nor did my tendency to knock on their door when I feared they had
overslept. "We're grown up, and if we're late for work, we'll take the consequences," Julia tells me one
morning after I'd roused them too early. I'm learning not to probe too deeply about physical ailments, job
interviews, or -- God forbid -- long-range life plans. "Don't make us into a project, Mom," she says.
It's her bad luck to have parents cursed with an instinctual gloomy outlook on life's vicissitudes. Years of
survival training as New Yorkers and newspaper reporters left us keenly aware of the snares and delusions

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
that lie in wait for the heedless. Naturally, our daughter grew up with an unflappable fearlessness that Silver
appears to share. Under the same roof, these opposing mind-sets can be combustible, as when we gently try
to temper what we view as Silver's obsolete immigrant's notion of American streets paved with gold. Most of
the time we come off as party poopers while failing to dampen his ebullience.
So I'm trying to stifle my natural impulse to meddle and worry. My silent, all-purpose mantra has become, "It's
none of your business" (as I squelch the urge to eavesdrop or opine on a decision they're grappling with). I
resist the temptation to pull out my checkbook and make their money problems go away. I've taught myself to
think of their cramped bedroom as an apartment in a distant city. That way, my feelings aren't hurt when they
repair to their quarters after dinner to be alone. And by giving them privacy, we gain our own: No longer
compelled to play hostess, I can retreat to our bedroom to read with a clear conscience.
The paradox: By returning to the fold, Julia is teaching me to let her go. Looking back, I don't think I ever
achieved this detente with my own parents. During visits after I'd left home, I remember snapping at my
mother for, well, being a mother: her frequent warnings about driving safely, the endless corrections as I tried
to cook a meal, and her habit of rearranging the dishes after I'd loaded them in the dishwasher. I'm getting my
comeuppance, for now it's Julia who critiques my kitchen techniques and rearranges the dishes in the
dishwasher.
Still, most of the friction has abated now that they've settled in. A bit of serendipity led Julia to an
apprenticeship with a professional equestrian who is teaching her to show and train horses -- a dream she
reluctantly put aside years ago as impractical. At first my husband was dubious, saying the horse business
was for rich people, and that she would spend more than she'd ever earn. But after she invited us to visit the
farm where she works, even he has had a change of heart. We see how fully engaged she is, working long
hours with obvious talent beside a mentor she admires.
As for Silver, he obtained a Social Security card and found work that offers medical benefits for both of them.
When I received that news, it felt like a giant boulder had magically lifted from my shoulders. (Relief was
short-lived; it turned out that for abysmal coverage the monthly premium equaled a full week's salary.)
Their schedules are so full we rarely see them except at dinner. Still, Julia and I have stolen a bit of time to
pick berries -- a favorite pastime when she was small. They fish with my husband and as a birthday gift
scraped and repainted his boat. They do most of the heavy lifting around the house and have produced some
memorable dinners.
We've become comfortable with our son-in-law in a way we never would have through brief visits and phone
calls. He banters with us like our own son and is clearly devoted to our daughter. He and Julia can share
holidays and milestones with us and are reconnecting with uncles, aunts and cousins she rarely saw when
she lived so far away. When Tucker finally succumbed to illness and old age, Julia thought to ask the vet to
euthanize him at home to spare him unnecessary stress. It was fitting to have her by our side as our beloved,
15-year-old family dog breathed his last.
Julia recently mentioned that they'd seen a farmhouse for rent nearer their jobs. When they go, as I know
they must, I'll tell our skeptical friends that freedom and privacy can't hold a candle to the messy, silly,
unpredictable, sometimes-painful fullness of family life. With any luck, they will put down roots within a day's
drive of here, so one day we can spoil any grandchildren-to-be.
I can't be sure, but I think we're growing on them.
---
Ms. Graham is a writer in Virginia. She can be reached at encore@wsj.com.

Gold, for Now, Departs From Norm
Commodities Report
Gold, for Now, Departs From Norm
By James Attwood Dow Jones Newswires
938 words
26 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SYDNEY, Australia -- Going by experience, gold's near-vertical gains and subsequent easing last week
would signal a sharp correction, but market watchers say economic uncertainties can still push the metal to
fresh highs in the months ahead.
Spot gold prices surged to 17-year highs last week, peaking at $475.45 an ounce Thursday as the metal's
haven status attracted investors concerned over the impact of high oil prices on U.S. economic growth and
global inflation in the wake of Hurricanes Katrina and Rita.
The rally, which defied gold's traditional role as a hedge against dollar weakness, appears to have subsided
as speculators booked profit when Hurricane Rita was downgraded to Category 4. Late Friday in the U.S.,
spot gold was trading around $463.75. September gold futures on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange fell $3.10 to $463.50. Some market watchers say further sustainable price gains first
require a more sizable speculative shakeout, indicating there will be more downward pressure on prices.
Analysts say the uncertainties driving gold are likely to linger, especially with Rita threatening U.S. energy
infrastructure. That keeps gold on track to hit $480 to $500 an ounce by year end, they say. That is a far cry
from the record London afternoon fix high of $850 set back in January 1980, not inflation adjusted, or about
$2,000 adjusted for inflation. The Comex high was $875, made on Jan. 12, 1980.
The expectation is U.S. economic growth will be materially weaker in the next 12 months as high oil prices act
as a brake and lower the end point for U.S. rate increases, said Commonwealth Bank of Australia commodity
strategist David Thurtell. That scenario has bearish implications for the U.S. dollar, he said, while Germany's
election means significant economic overhauls are unlikely in the next few years, with the euro suffering
accordingly.
"People are saying the euro has got some problems, but we don't want to go back into U.S. dollar [which] has
its own problems, so they're saying gold's worth a go on its own merits," Mr. Thurtell said.
In addition, gold appears well supported fundamentally with the prospect of central banks such as those of
Argentina and Russia stepping up gold purchases; unusually strong physical demand from India in the
festival season starting mid-October; and sluggish mine production expected in the Middle East.
A recent research article from J.P. Morgan suggests gold's strength is underpinned by China's appetite for
luxury goods, which is still in its infancy, and could support gold demand for many years. Furthermore, it said
mine expansion by major gold producer South Africa is leveling off. Robust physical demand and gold's
growing status as an alternative asset class for portfolio holdings are expected to continue providing strong
support on price drops, analysts said. "In the wake of Hurricane Katrina and now Rita, we expect gold to go
as high as $495 in the next few months," Mr. Thurtell said.
The market is expected to maintain a wait-and-see-approach to the hurricanes' impact on oil prices and the
U.S. economy and how European central banks react when their agreed gold selling period resumes
tomorrow. Gordon Cheung, precious metals director at Mitsui Bussan Hong Kong, says he expects prices to
stay capped at $475 in the near term, with a possibility of further correction. Mr. Cheung agrees the market
should be back around $490 toward year's end, driven by an anticipated weakness in the U.S. dollar as the
Federal Reserve adopts a less-aggressive approach on raising interest rates against a backdrop of
persistent economic uncertainties.
According to Darren Heathcote, head of trading at N M Rothschild &Sons (Australia) Ltd., inflationary support
for gold will ebb and flow over the next few months until oil prices ease back toward $50 a barrel. "But with
hurricanes continuing to threaten, it wouldn't [be surprising] to see oil pushing over $70 [a barrel] again, and
that's going to have a push on gold," he said.

--- Page 8 ---
Page 8 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Other analysts also see gold's recent rally as driven by economic factors that eventually will catch up with the
dollar, in turn providing further support for the metal, as its dollar correlation is far stronger for rising gold and
a weakening dollar than the other way around.
"Now is a very pivotal phase because there are a lot of events in the market. But once we're in the next phase
in terms of the U.S. dollar weakening, that's when you'll see solidification in terms of gold moving towards
$500," said Alastair McIntyre, precious-metals director at ScotiaMocatta Hong Kong.
Not everyone, however, is convinced that gold can, or should, maintain its upward momentum. While the
long-term outlook remains bullish, the recent rally is unsustainable without a more-sizable speculative
shakeout, according to Sean Russo, a director of Sydney-based risk advisory and funds management firm,
Noah's Rule.
Supportive sentiment surrounding the metal is at almost unprecedented highs but most of the buying is
technical or momentum-driven, he said. "There's a lot of fresh money coming into gold but it isn't necessarily
smart money [and] can go out again just as fast," Mr. Russo said. "There is a high likelihood that this week or
next we're going to see a price that we're not going to see exceeded for the rest of the year."

Fighting Inflation with Wood? --- Firms Start Pushing `Hard Asset' Funds as Price Protection
Personal Finance
Fighting Inflation with Wood? --- Firms Start Pushing `Hard Asset' Funds as Price Protection
By Erin E. Arvedlund
1,458 words
24 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WITH INFLATION worries again in the air, investment firms are pushing a new spate of "hard asset" funds.
These funds invest in physical assets, such as timber and real estate, with the goal of hedging against a
possible surge in the cost of living.
Aimed at wealthy investors, the new funds have high minimum-investment levels and can lock up investors'
money in illiquid assets for many years. But the idea is to diversify one's portfolio to include products -- from
agricultural products to energy holdings -- that tend to outperform in times of inflation, which can ravage a
traditional portfolio of stocks and bonds.
Inflation fears have increased in the wake of Hurricane Katrina and the onset of Rita: Energy prices have
soared and the rebuilding efforts are expected to stoke demand for many materials. The Federal Reserve
indicated this week that it would continue to raise interest rates to ward off inflationary pressures.
People think "inflation could break out suddenly, and in that environment, financial assets often do poorly,"
says Greg Curtis, president of Greycourt &Co., a Pittsburgh wealth-management boutique. "We're seeing
much more serious interest in real assets." He said his firm is "nudging clients to get at least a little of their
portfolio into" the investments.
To be sure, there are many lower-cost, easily traded investment vehicles that act as inflation hedges,
including mutual funds such as Pimco Real Estate Real Return Strategy, available since October 2003, which
can invest in real-estate-linked derivatives. It has returned 30.95% over the past 12 months, according to
Morningstar, which tracks fund performance. Morningstar lists more than 50 Specialty Natural Resource
mutual funds, such as Pimco All Asset and Oppenheimer Real Asset. The category has returned 38.22%
year to date and 17.60% annually over five years. The lowest-cost options are exchange-traded funds that
track indexes such as gold or agricultural commodities.
These commodity mutual funds traditionally hedge against inflation mainly by buying derivatives, such as
futures and options, that are based on underlying physical assets. While liquid, derivatives provide only
short-term inflation protection.
By contrast, the new crop of funds coming on the market, mainly from boutique firms, also invest in
derivatives, but there is an increased emphasis on owning physical assets directly, which gives them a longer
time horizon as an inflation hedge.
Investments in what are commonly referred to as real-asset, or hard-asset, funds have in the past mainly
been available only to pension funds and endowments. Today, minimum investments in some new funds
geared to "high net worth" individuals start at $500,000, although most require larger initial investments of
between $1 million and $5 million.
Management fees at hard-asset funds are in the 1% to 2% range, but the funds also take a cut of the returns
that could amount to 20% or more. Advisers recommend investors limit such investments to no more than 5%
to 10% of one's portfolio.
Some financial advisers warn that the time to invest in real assets may have passed, following at least three
years of surging real estate, oil and other commodities prices such as steel and gold. But proponents of
these funds say that certain hard assets still have an upside. "Today, each sector in the hard-asset space has
its own supply and demand trends," says Mark Cirilli, chief investment officer for Marshall Street
Management, South Norwalk, Conn., which invests on behalf of Jesse Fink, a co-founder of Priceline.com.
For example, Mr. Cirilli says strong demand already has lowered yields on timber assets to about 6% to 8%
from about 12% in past years. Instead, he says he likes certain energy plays, such as clean fuels like
biomass and solar power.

--- Page 12 ---
Page 12 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Bessemer Trust's $800 million Old Westbury Real Return Fund, launched in April, is adding to its holdings in
agricultural products and gold, says Marc D. Stern, Bessemer's chief investment officer. "While these
decisions were not dictated by Hurricanes Katrina or Rita, the inflationary effects of reconstruction reinforce
the investment potential of these holdings," he says.
With such large amounts of money at stake, and assets likely to be locked up for years, prospective investors
need to be especially careful in performing due diligence on hard-asset funds.
"Real assets have seen some explosive returns, but they're quite hard to invest in with confidence and
comfort, in part because due diligence is difficult," says John Griswold, executive director of Commonfund
Institute, the Wilton, Conn., research arm of Commonfund, which manages money for nonprofit organizations
and colleges. He says Commonfund hires independent appraisers to conduct research on how each
real-asset fund prices its holdings and whether performance returns are accurate.
Interest by individuals seems to be picking up. Prince &Associates, a private-wealth consulting firm in
Shelton, Conn., found in a survey that investors with $1 million to $20 million in net worth expect to increase
their allocation in hard assets to 40% in 2006. That is up from 34% this year and 23% when the firm began
the survey in 2003. Prince surveyed nearly 500 investors who already invest directly in hard assets.
Steven Braverman, a former options trader who runs his own family office, says he considers real estate and
energy holdings as "direct ownership of inflationary hedges to our conventional investment portfolio." He says
he recently added holdings of light-industrial property and optioned land with natural-gas deposits.
One new hard-asset fund is Walton Street Real Estate Fund V, scheduled to launch this month by Walton
Street Capital, a private-equity firm in Chicago whose founders include Neil Bluhm, the former head of JMB
Realty Corp. Returns on earlier Walton Street funds have averaged 25% annually by buying properties such
as foreclosed office space in Seattle. The new fund plans to seek similar opportunities.
To be sure, there are simpler ways to invest in real estate, including buying shares of publicly traded real
estate investment trusts, or REITs, which offer regular payouts and certain tax advantages. But REIT prices
tend to align themselves more closely with major stock indices, and less with their underlying assets.
Therefore, proponents of owning real estate say that strategy provides a better inflation hedge.
Sterling Stamos Real Assets Fund, a so-called fund of funds with a target size of $250 million, also is
scheduled to launch this month. It will focus on hedge funds and private-equity funds that buy real estate,
natural resources and operating assets such as cellphone towers. Funds of funds are composed of a variety
of underlying funds and charge an additional management fee.
Robeco Sustainable Private Equity Fund, launched in the spring, invests in clean-fuel technologies such as
wind-power generation, as do the new Expansion Capital and Draper Fisher Jurvetson's venture capital fund
Element. Alerian Opportunity, an energy hedge fund launched in June and run by John Fullerton, formerly a
managing director at J.P. Morgan, lowered the investment minimum to $500,000 from $1 million. Two new
timber funds available to individual, ORM Timber, which has timber holdings in the Pacific Northwest, and
Molpus Woodlands Fund, with holdings in the Southeast, closed in August. Molpus says its earlier funds,
which it began offering to institutional investors in 1997, have returned 11.3% annually.
---
Investing in Hard Assets
Hard-asset funds, which are attracting renewed interest from wealthy
investors, invest directly in such industries as real estate, energy, and
timber. Minimums and fees are steep. Here is a sampling of some of these
funds and their strategies:

Investors Stay on a Storm Watch
Thursday's Markets
Investors Stay on a Storm Watch
By E.S. Browning
490 words
23 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IN THE FINANCIAL MARKETS, it was all Rita, all the time.
Yesterday, the news improved, as Hurricane Rita was downgraded to a Category 4 storm from Category 5.
Oil prices fell. Stocks rebounded a bit after three days of declines that had knocked 2.5% off the Dow Jones
Industrial Average and pushed it to a two-month low.
Although investors remained jittery and kept their computers glued to maps of the hurricane's course, the
Dow finished up 44.02 points, or 0.42%, at 10422.05. The blue-chip average still is down 2.1% for the week,
and off 3.4% this year.
"Everybody has that Web site up, the National Hurricane Center Web site. Everybody is watching the strike
probability and whether it is Category 4 or Category 5," said Matthew Johnson, head of stock syndication at
Lehman Brothers.
Reflecting traders' concern, the New York Mercantile Exchange, which trades oil futures, announced that it
will open certain electronic trading as early as 10 a.m. EDT on Sunday, nine hours early.
Some investors were impressed that stocks haven't suffered more with the Federal Reserve raising interest
rates and two hurricanes hitting the nation's Gulf oil facilities. That guarded optimism contributed to the stock
recovery. Rita also appeared to be veering eastward, creating hopes that it might miss some of the main
refining sites.
At the same time, the American Petroleum Institute, a trade group, warned that 28% of U.S. refining capacity
lay in the hurricane's potential path. Few investors were sticking their necks out far until they could see how
severely oil production and refining are hurt. Although most stock indexes were up, more stocks fell than rose
on both the New York Stock Exchange and Nasdaq.
Wal-Mart Stores, DuPont and Verizon Communications all fell during the day to new lows for the year and
then rebounded. Wal-Mart finished with a gain and DuPont and Verizon finished with small declines. Alcoa
and General Electric both neared their lows for the year, before rebounding.
Oil futures and gold both fell on the day, as some investors took profits and as fears moderated.
After regular trading, however, Alcoa warned that high energy prices and softer aluminum prices will hurt
earnings. The stock fell 5% in after-hours trading.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.37%, or 4.42 points, to 1214.62, now up 0.22% for 2005. The
Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.20%, or 4.14 points, to 2110.78, down 3% this year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks and bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 1/32, or 31 cents for each $1,000 invested,
pushing the yield down to 4.180%.
The dollar strengthened, to 111.73 yen, up from 111.32; the euro fell to $1.2151 from $1.2210.

Sailing Past Katrina
Sailing Past Katrina
By David Malpass
1,220 words
22 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
No dollar value can be put on the deaths, suffering and dislocation due to Hurricane Katrina and the broken
levees in New Orleans. But we can quantify the bad news in GDP terms and try to look ahead.
In the very short run, U.S. output declined after Katrina hit shore. Factories and businesses closed in the
affected areas and electric power, a major component of industrial production, stopped flowing to millions of
users. We know that Katrina, which struck Aug. 29, reduced August industrial production by roughly 0.3%
($717 million), with a deeper hit expected in the September data. Hundreds of thousands may have been out
of jobs in the first weeks of this month, and high-frequency data is beginning to show this impact already.
There were 68,000 first-wave Katrina-related jobless claims in the week ended Sept. 10, and August retail
sales weakened some due to store closures.
The payroll survey due on Oct. 7 will probably show a net loss of jobs in September, the first such net job loss
since May 2003. Both job gains due to rebuilding and especially job losses due to Katrina may run well above
the norm. Gross job gains averaged 2.62 million per month in 2004, while gross job losses averaged 2.45
million.
The parallel household survey, also due on Oct. 7, may not be quite as gloomy. The average monthly
increase in jobs so far in 2005 has been 287,000 per the household survey, versus 194,000 in the payroll
survey, which survey has shown a pattern of upward revisions when there's a disagreement between it and
the household data. The government's September phone inquiries for the household survey won't work in
New Orleans. Instead, guesswork or footnotes will have to be used to handle the evacuees, many of whom
may consider themselves employed, in fact hard at work, in a temporary location not captured in the payroll
survey.
The result might leave the two surveys split, one up, one down, reflecting the reality of an economy in which
job growth is rapid but hard to document at known businesses. The September unemployment rate (which is
based on the household survey) will likely rise from August's 4.9%, but the rate should still remain relatively
low by historical standards.
September's industrial production report, released in mid-October, will show the magnitude of the hit to
manufacturing and utility output. Harder to discern in advance, September's retail sales data in mid-October
will include both the closure of New Orleans but also the tenacious rebuilding and restocking effort likely
across the Gulf Coast in the second half of the month.
GDP data won't directly record the storm's wallop to the nation's infrastructure, homes and buildings. The
actual destruction of an asset is not counted in GDP, though the loss of rents and home usage will subtract a
bit. By economic definition, "gross domestic product" records the creation of goods, services, homes and
machines, not their abrupt destruction. Depreciation subtracts them gradually from GDP at an average rate
based on normal wear and tear. In contrast, rebuilding efforts are counted in the investment component of
GDP as they are built. So rebuilding should add materially to GDP in the fourth quarter and in 2006.
Thus the short-term effect of Katrina on GDP statistics will probably be negative in the third quarter and
positive in the fourth, leaving the second half growth rate at about the same strong 3.5%-4% that has
prevailed since the 2003 tax cut.
While some expect a substantial economic slowdown, neither Katrina nor high energy prices are likely to be
the trigger. The economy had momentum and liquidity going into the storm and, with interest rates rising
apace, will probably continue its strong and relatively steady growth path into 2006. Gold's mid-September
move above $460, after talk of a Fed pause in rate hikes, highlights the Katrina-related inflation risk, but also
the responsiveness of the price of gold to rate expectations.

--- Page 16 ---
Page 16 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
True, post-Katrina rebuilding will test capacity constraints for many products and workers. But the rebuilding
in the south should peak in the winter months when construction is slower in the north, smoothing the
constraints. Expensive energy, rather than being the cause of the slump many have been predicting, is more
likely the result of faster-than-expected U.S. and Asian growth. After an initial Katrina-related spike, the
unemployment rate should push back below August's 4.9% rate, providing a burst of new income, plus
confidence in the economy's flexibility and resilience.
The more likely cause of the next prolonged slowdown or recession will be overheating and moderate
inflation, met by higher interest rates and then moderation in the growth of consumption, housing and
corporate profits. However, with government spending red hot, inventories low, rate hikes not excessive and
foreign growth strong, a restrictive level of interest rates and a traditional slowdown are probably a while off.
When the Fed does take the punch bowl away, the price of some stocks and houses will decline, but others
probably won't, leaving the market with the devil-in-the-details challenge of picking which ones fall, when, and
from what peak.
By 2007 or 2008, higher interest rates will have removed the monetary stimulus, while fiscal stimulus will
have added to GDP but not to the longer-term growth potential. Thus, structural reforms of the federal tax,
spending and regulatory processes will be critical in avoiding a substantial slowdown. Tax increases,
automatic in 2008 and 2010 unless new laws are passed, will be at the top of the market's worry-list, and the
key variable in the depth of the slowdown.
---
It's still possible that 2006 will produce a major, pro-growth tax reform that will invite productive investment for
years to come. At this point it doesn't seem likely. Congress's arcane scoring rules explicitly assume that tax
reform won't improve the economic growth rate, ignoring any federal revenue gains that might come from
improving the tax system. This makes reform politically difficult. Katrina's additions to Uncle Sam's $2.4 trillion
annual spending further complicate the deficit debate, lowering the odds of tax reform and adding to the risk
of a 2007 or 2008 slowdown.
Thus, Katrina's lasting economic effect, rather than altering the growth path by much, will be tangential. But it
may weaken Washington's important drive for tax, spending and other structural reforms. The durability of the
current economic expansion is the result of low real interest rates from Sept. 11 to the present, the decline in
the value of the dollar in 2002 and 2003 ending the deflation, the 2003 tax cut, and above all the flexibility of
the U.S. economic system -- its small businesses, workers, innovation and capital structure.
If strong growth resumes post-Katrina and extends into 2006, it won't be an argument for hurricanes or
government rebuilding programs. It will be a tribute to the economy's often-underestimated sturdiness, and an
urgent admonition to push ahead with growth-oriented structural reforms.
---
Mr. Malpass is chief economist at Bear Stearns.

Stocks Slide on Hurricane Rita Fears; Bonds Rise Over Economic Concerns
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Slide on Hurricane Rita Fears; Bonds Rise Over Economic Concerns
By E.S. Browning
438 words
22 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal

THE DOW JONES Industrial Average slipped to its lowest level in more than two months as Hurricane Rita
pushed toward coastal Texas's oil platforms and refineries, forcing crude and gasoline prices higher and
stoking fears that consumer spending will suffer.
With investors again consulting weather forecasts and maps of oil installations, the Dow industrials suffered a
third consecutive decline, falling 103.49 points, or 0.99%, to 10378.03. It was the lowest finish since July 7,
leaving the blue chips down 3.8% this year and off 2.5% this week.
Bond prices rose on fears of an economic slowdown, which would hold longer-term interest rates down,
making existing bonds more attractive.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, which had been up for the year, fell into the red, down 0.1% for 2005.
For the day, it retreated 0.91%, or 11.14 points, to 1210.20.
Fueling the consumer-spending worries, Avon Products lowered its profit forecast for the year, hurting stocks
of other consumer-product companies. Avon fell 11.8%. Wal-Mart Stores fell 1.7%.
Some investors bought downtrodden stocks, figuring the market was overreacting based on raw memories of
Katrina. With gasoline prices back above $3 a gallon at many stations, fears spread that spending in the
make-or-break holiday season could suffer. "I saw enough trading volume that I think people are changing
their view, at least short-term. They are becoming concerned, until they get new data, about what the
Christmas season is going to look like," said Robert Harrington, head of U.S. stock trading at UBS's
brokerage arm.
Investors were disappointed at the Federal Reserve's indication Tuesday that inflation remains a danger and
that policy makers intend to keep pushing up short-term interest rates.
Oil futures rose 60 cents to $66.80 a barrel, still below the 22-year high of $69.81 on Aug. 30. Gold rose
$2.70 to $468.90 an ounce, another 17-year high. The Nasdaq composite fell 1.2%, or 24.69 points, to
2106.64, off 3.2% this year. In major market action:
Stocks retreated. On the NYSE, where 1.93 billion shares traded, 1,106 stocks rose and 2,201 fell.

Why Belgians Shoot Horses in Texas For Dining in Europe --- Grass-Fed Meat Is Superior, But Slaughterhouses Draw Growing Criticism in U.S.
Why Belgians Shoot Horses in Texas For Dining in Europe --- Grass-Fed Meat Is Superior, But
Slaughterhouses Draw Growing Criticism in U.S.
By Mary Jacoby
1,335 words
21 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TOURCOING, France -- Christian Dhalluin, a butcher in this rural French hamlet near the Belgian border,
dropped some ground meat into a bowl and mixed it with a spicy mayonnaise sauce to make his specialty:
American horse meat tartare.
"I love America," said Mr. Dhalluin. "The horse meat from the U.S. is the best in the world."
Some Americans would be distressed to hear that. A vocal antislaughter movement argues that horses have
a special place in American culture and history and should not be killed for food. Activists have spurred an
energetic but uphill effort in Congress to shut down the last three horse slaughterhouses in the U.S. All are
Belgian-owned and supply butchers around the world.
A U.S. ban would mean that Mr. Dhalluin would no longer be able to buy the meat that vaulted him to a gold
medal in a recent culinary contest for "best sausage in the category of garlic."
"Americans do not profit from slaughtering horses," Rep. John Sweeney, a New York Republican trying to
close down the slaughterhouses, said in House debate in June. "Foreigners eat our horses, and foreign
companies make money off the sale of meat."
The revelation three years ago that the 1986 Kentucky Derby winner, Ferdinand, ended up in a
slaughterhouse in Japan, galvanized the U.S. antislaughter movement -- and caused two of the
Belgian-owned plants to take on lawyers and lobbyists. "Toss in Mr. Ed and Black Beauty, and we have a real
public-relations problem," says Olivier Kemseke, a Belgian horse-meat dealer whose family owns one of the
Texas slaughterhouses under attack.
Federal law doesn't ban eating horse in the U.S., but the meat is now no longer sold for human consumption
domestically. It was marketed during the meat shortages of World War II. A lack of demand later dried up the
domestic market, though horse meat remained on the menu of the Harvard Faculty Club in Cambridge,
Mass., until 1983. The chef took it off when he could no longer get fresh meat; the steaks were arriving
frozen.
The U.S. Department of Agriculture, which inspects the horses headed for foreign tables, says 58,736 horses
were slaughtered in the U.S. last year for human consumption, yielding 13.6 million pounds of meat for export
to the European Union, Japan, Mexico and Switzerland. A decade ago, there were around a dozen U.S.
facilities slaughtering horses for export. Today, with demand declining, that's down to just two in Texas and
one in Illinois.
Mr. Kemseke, 33 years old, is the third generation of his family to be in the horse-meat business. He owns
slaughterhouses in New Zealand and Romania but likes the American quarter horse best. Ample grazing land
means more American horses eat natural grasses, enhancing their flavor, he says.
In the 1990s, Mr. Kemseke lived in Kaufman, Texas, where he managed the family's U.S. slaughterhouse. He
loved the ranching town, pop. 6,700, near Dallas. "I had a little cowboy thing going," he says, slapping his
pants and shirt. "Wrangler jeans, the belt, the boots, the Western shirt." He cruised around town in a 1971
brown Cadillac. "Everybody waved and called my name. I was living the American life."
Foes of horse slaughter portray the meat as an exotic delicacy for foreigners, evoking images of Paris
brasseries serving up American horse meat alongside foie gras and champagne. But many consumers of
horse meat are more like Nicole Chaupin, a French homemaker in a skirt and sneakers who ordered a small
container of Mr. Dhalluin's freshly made horse tartare. "It's good. It's healthy," Mrs. Chaupin said of horse
meat, which is slightly redder than beef, more tender and gamier in flavor.

--- Page 20 ---
Page 20 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Historically, consumption of horse meat in Europe was associated with poverty and desperation. The practice
is believed to have begun when Napoleon's troops, fighting the Russians at the Battle of Eylau in Poland in
1807, ran low on supplies and ate their horses. Horse meat helped sustain Europeans during the deprivations
of two World Wars.
Because horse meat is high in iron and low in fat, European doctors today often prescribe it to treat anemia.
The American Quarter Horse Association, the American Veterinary Medical Association and other groups
support slaughter, arguing that there are not enough rescue facilities to care for unwanted horses. And in
Washington, many farm-state lawmakers also want to keep the slaughterhouses open, in part because
closing them might embolden animal-rights groups and vegetarians to demand a ban on the slaughter of beef
cattle, pigs and sheep. "What is the distinction between a steer, a hog and a horse?" Iowa Rep. Steve King
asked on the House floor in June. The zebra he ate in Africa last year was excellent, the Iowa Republican
said.
Horses "are not like other animals," says John Hettinger, a thoroughbred breeder and auction-house owner in
Saratoga Springs, N.Y. "I've seen a Clydesdale without a halter on performing intricate maneuvers in
Madison Square Garden," he says. "Now, I'd like a cattleman to show me a cow that can do the same thing."
Mr. Hettinger, 72, has spent $160,000 on Washington lobbyists in an effort to ban the slaughter of horses,
federal records show. The thoroughbred auction house he controls, Fasig-Tipton Inc., once sold Man o' War,
whose racing career from 1919 to 1920 is considered one of the greatest in American history. "I've made my
living off horses," says Mr. Hettinger, "and this is my way of giving back." The Texas plants have spent about
the same amount in an effort to preserve it, according to Mr. Kemseke. The Texas slaughterhouses' lobbyist,
Jim Bradshaw, has made more than $27,000 in campaign donations to pro-slaughter lawmakers, federal
records show.
While the debate goes on, an American Airlines flight takes off every day from Dallas/Fort Worth International
Airport, headed for Paris's Charles DeGaulle airport with a load of horse carcasses in its cargo belly. After
passing French inspection, the U.S. horse meat from Mr. Kemseke's plant is driven in refrigerated trucks to
rural Rekkem, Belgium, where it is repackaged and shipped to butchers. Some especially choosy butchers,
like Mr. Dhalluin, come to the plant's freezers to pick their own cuts.
Mr. Kemseke uses local horses, too. In a run-down neighborhood in central Brussels called Anderlecht,
famous for its abattoirs, Mr. Kemseke watched recently as 200 frightened Belgian horses were unloaded from
trailers, kicking and snorting, and tied to iron rails. Wearing a butcher's robe, he walked among the animals,
lifting tails and slapping flanks, making notes on a pad about which to purchase for slaughter.
Horses are slaughtered in the same manner as cattle: with a metal bolt shot into their heads. The
antislaughter activists call this method particularly inhumane. "If our friends in Belgium want to eat horse
meat, I'm not trying to dictate that they do or not do it," says Skip Trimble of the Texas Humane Legislation
Network. "But we in America, who view the horse differently, should not supply them with our horses."
So far, economic arguments have prevailed over the emotional appeals of the antislaughter forces. Mr.
Bradshaw, the slaughterhouse lobbyist, tells lawmakers the Texas plants spend $6 million a year shipping
horse meat with American Airlines and other U.S. carriers.
Even the oversized American flag at the American Legion post that greets drivers entering Kaufman is paid
for by Mr. Kemseke's horse-slaughter business. "So they want to close us down?" Mr. Kemseke says. "Then I
don't know where Kaufman's gonna get their next flag."
(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: The Pros and Cons of Eating Horses" -- WSJ Oct. 1, 2005)

Greenspan and Katrina
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Greenspan and Katrina
503 words
20 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Along the way in his long career, Alan Greenspan has been known to follow the price of gold. Not as a gold
bug who thinks it is the lodestar of monetary policy, but as one who finds it a useful indicator of inflationary
expectations. We hope he's following the barbaric metal that way now, because yesterday it pushed through
$467 an ounce at the close in London, to a 17-year high.
This gold surge is probably a response to the speculation that the Federal Reserve might actually make the
mistake at its meeting today of pausing in its monetary tightening in the wake of Hurricane Katrina. Yields on
the 10-year note have also climbed back up from their Katrina lows, and even stripping out the Katrina energy
spike, prices on steel, lumber, cement and other goods have been rising.
Which means easing up now would be exactly the wrong policy. Those urging such an action include
carry-traders who fear getting caught playing the yield curve, investors long on housing, and all kinds of
well-intentioned folks who simply don't want to see the expansion end. We know how they feel. This market
psychology always takes hold amid a Fed-tightening cycle -- and this time it is being exaggerated by the
fallout from Katrina.
But the Fed cannot fine-tune economic growth. It has only one job -- maintaining price stability. We've been
writing for two years that the Fed has been providing too much liquidity, with negative real interest rates, and
even its 250-basis point rise over the past 15 months has not brought rates back to neutrality. Stopping the
rate increases now would run the risk of even greater increases later, as price pressures continue to build.
Our guess is that this is not the kind of policy dilemma Mr. Greenspan wants to leave his successor in 2006
and 2007. We also doubt he wants to depart in January having abandoned some of the hard-earned progress
against inflation that he and his predecessor Paul Volcker earned since the last time we had an oil shock, in
the 1970s. The Fed printed dollars to offset the economic damage from rising oil prices, only to create more
inflation and still higher oil prices. The inflationary cycle didn't end until Ronald Reagan backed Mr. Volcker's
slam on the brakes.
Katrina's blow will reduce economic output in the third quarter and maybe the fourth, but probably without
inducing recession. The economy entered the eye of the hurricane with momentum built up over two years of
4% average GDP growth since the tax cuts of 2003. The rebuilding of the Gulf Coast will again lift GDP as it
gains momentum later this year and into next. A rate hike now could actually have the beneficial effect of
calming inflation fears and keeping long-term interest rates lower. If the Fed keeps prices stable, the animal
spirits of the private sector will do the rest.

New Storm Ignites Oil Prices, Damps Stocks
Monday's Markets
New Storm Ignites Oil Prices, Damps Stocks
By E.S. Browning
454 words
20 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MOVING TOWARD the hurricane-ravaged Gulf of Mexico, a new storm named Rita sent oil futures soaring
and knocked 84.31 points off the Dow Jones Industrial Average amid renewed concern for refining and oil
production in the region.
Having initially underestimated Hurricane Katrina, investors preferred to err on the other side. They pushed
crude-oil futures up $4.39, or 7%, to $67.39 a barrel, the sharpest one-day percentage gain since 2001 and
the biggest dollar gain since oil began trading on the New York Mercantile Exchange in 1983. Gasoline,
natural-gas and heating-oil futures all rose more than 10%. Despite a gain by Exxon Mobil, the Dow
industrials overall fell 0.79% to 10557.63, down 2.1% this year.
"When people saw how bad Katrina was, and the impacts it had on the economy and the energy industry and
everything else, you knew the next thing coming through the Gulf wasn't going to catch anyone by surprise,"
said Michael Driscoll, who trades energy stocks at New York brokerage firm Bear Stearns.
Among the few stocks to benefit were those involved in energy production and services. Energy stocks now
represent 10% of the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index for the first time since 1994, and Exxon has
surpassed $400 billion in market value, the fourth stock ever to do that, Standard &Poor's said. (The other
three, General Electric, Microsoft and Cisco Systems, subsequently fell below that threshold.)
Reflecting fears of inflation and economic disruption, gold futures jumped 1.6% to $466.70 an ounce,
another 17-year high. On top of the storm jitters, investors were nervous about today's Federal Reserve
meeting. Policy makers will decide whether to push their target overnight lending rate to 3.75% from 3.5%.
The S&P 500 fell 0.56%, or 6.89 points, to 1231.02, still up 1.6% this year. The Nasdaq Composite Index
declined 0.70%, or 15.09 points, to 2145.26, down 1.4% on the year.
The dollar rose, as political confusion in Germany hurt the euro.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, 1.55 billion shares traded; 1,062 stocks rose, and 2,211 fell.
Bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 5/32, or $1.56 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 4.250%. The 30-year bond was up 4/32 to yield 4.550%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 111.48 yen, up from 111.36, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2151 from $1.2234.

Stocks Rally, Nearly Erasing Week's Declines
Friday's Markets
Stocks Rally, Nearly Erasing Week's Declines
By Peter A. McKay
418 words
17 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE STOCK MARKET SHOOK off data suggesting deep Katrina-related jitters among consumers to finish
higher on the day, aided by a drop in energy prices and talk of big spending on storm recovery.
The rally tempered -- but didn't erase -- losses for the week in major stock indexes.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 83.19 points, or 0.8%, to 10641.94, off 0.3% on the week. Analyst
upgrades boosted two of its component stocks: Exxon Mobil and Intel.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index climbed 10.18 points, or 0.8%, to end at 1237.91, off 0.3% on the
week. The Nasdaq Composite Index gained 0.7%, or 14.20 points, to 2160.35, off 0.7% on the week.
On the year, the S&P has eked out a small gain, up 2.1%.
Investors reacted to President Bush's televised speech Thursday night, with his new, sweeping promises of
federal assistance to rebuild the devastated Gulf Coast. Analysts now expect spending to exceed $200
billion.
On what would usually be a downbeat note for stocks, the University of Michigan's monthly
consumer-confidence index plummeted to 76.9 from 89.1 in August, the lowest reading in 13 years.
However, the report sparked worries about reduced demand for energy, resulting in a selloff of energy
futures, which aided stocks. Crude-oil futures contracts for October delivery fell 2.7%, or $1.75, to $63 a
barrel, off 1.7% on the week at the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Gold jumped to 17-year highs, aided by bets that spending on Gulf Coast reconstruction may spark inflation.
October futures gained 0. 9%, or $4, to end at $459.50 a troy ounce.
The outcome of Tuesday's meeting of the Federal Reserve's rate-setting committee should sharpen investors'
economic outlook.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. On the New York Stock Exchange, where more than 2.4 billion shares traded, 1,325 stocks
declined and 1,949 rose.
Bond prices fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 13/32, or $4.0625 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
up to 4.267%. The 30-year bond fell 22/32 to yield 4.557%.
The dollar was mixed. In New York, it traded at 111.36 yen, up from 110.67 yen, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.2234 from $1.2224.

Investors Bullish on Nikkei Despite a Stumble
World Stock Markets
Investors Bullish on Nikkei Despite a Stumble
By Kanji Ishibashi Dow Jones Newswires
659 words
17 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B6
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TOKYO -- The Nikkei Stock Average fell after the 13000 barrier proved too tough to crack, but demand for
banks, brokerage houses and steel makers created a solid floor and indicated bullish sentiment still reigns.
"The underlying market trend is strong," said Masayuki Kubota, a fund manager at Daiwa SB Investments.
The Nikkei average of 225 shares shed 28.10 points, or 0.2%, to 12958.68, giving up some ground on
profit-taking after a 152.53-point rise Thursday. The broader Topix index of all Tokyo Stock Exchange First
Section issues gained 1.55 points, or 0.1%, to 1328.84, building on a 14.94-point gain Thursday.
Mr. Kubota said the bullish mood stems from a growing sense among investors that strengthening demand in
Japan, not overseas, will drive Japan's economic expansion. That is helping the Tokyo stock market separate
from movements in New York shares, he said.
Based on that view, foreign investors bought a wide range of shares, traders said.
Sony rose 1.2% to 4,060 yen (US$36.69) after the Nihon Keizai Shimbun reported yesterday that the
company is considering selling its financial operations so it can better focus on reviving its electronics
business. Sony denied the report. But hopes for big changes at the electronics maker are rising ahead of its
business-strategy briefing scheduled for Sept. 22.
Persistent hopes for a durable recovery in Japan's economy underpinned buying of financial stocks. Major
banks also benefited after Moody's Investors Service said it has placed its financial-strength ratings of banks
on review for a possible upgrade. Sumitomo Mitsui Financial Group rose 2.2% to 965,000 yen and Nomura
Holdings gained 2.1% to 1,577 yen.
But caution over the fast pace of the Tokyo stock market's gains and the U.S. central bank's
monetary-policy meeting set for next week spurred profit-taking in real-estate, oil and technology stocks.
Many traders expect the Nikkei 225 next week to attempt to top its psychological barrier of 13000, a level
untouched since June 26, 2001. But some are cautious.
In LONDON, miners showed their mettle, closing higher and taking the benchmark FTSE index along with
them, as spot gold prices hit highs. The FTSE 100 index closed up 0.5% at 5407.90, breaking above the
5400 mark for the first time in four years. Mining majors Rio Tinto and Anglo American had strong gains for a
second day. Supermarket operator William Morrison gained 1.7% on hopes that a strike over the possible
closure of some distribution centers could be avoided.
In FRANKFURT, Germany's benchmark DAX index flirted with a key resistance level and closed higher
before Sunday's federal election, while other indexes took support from a stronger start to U.S. trading and
lower crude. The German DAX Xetra 30 finished up 1.6% at 4986.50, just off the key 5000 mark, after
declining for most of the week. Polls point to opposition leader Angela Merkel unseating Chancellor Gerhard
Schroeder, but uncertainty remains about the composition of the next government.

The mood of U.S. consumers plunged this month as they absorbed the impact of Hurricane Katrina and
higher energy prices, according to the University of Michigan's mid-month report on consumer sentiment.
But oil recoiled, greatly helping investor sentiment.
The October crude futures contract on the New York Mercantile Exchange settled $1.75 lower at $63 a barrel,
the lowest settlement price since Aug. 5. Crude is at pre-Hurricane Katrina levels for the first time since the
storm.

and oil, as well as strong demand. Canyon Resources (Amex) advanced six cents, or 8.8%, to 74 cents;
Stillwater Mining gained 61 cents, or 6.7%, to 9.69; and Hecla Mining strengthened by 53 cents, or 14%, to
4.43, the latter two on the New York Stock Exchange.

2.18, or 6.9%, to 33.57; Greater Bay Bancorp rose 74 cents, or 3%, to 25.81; and OceanFirst Financial rose
97 cents, or 4.2%, to 24.10.
Cavalier Homes jumped 90 cents, or 13%, to 8.04 on the Amex. In one of the first contracts of its type, the
manufactured housing producer reported an agreement to build 2,000 homes as part of the Federal
Emergency Management Agency's disaster-relief effort for Gulf Coast states after Katrina.
But otherwise, home builder were in the basement as the day's worst performing group. Nobility Homes lost
1.36, or 4.3%, to 30.22; William Lyon Homes (NYSE) fell 6.45, or 3.9%, to 157.55; and M/I Homes (NYSE)
fell 90 cents, or 1.6%, to 55.30. Sentiment was said to be rising that the Federal Reserve will raise interest
rates when it meets next week, after earlier feelings about a pause after Hurricane Katrina.
On Friday, the Russell 2000 Index rose 6.56, or 0.99%, to 671.98. For the week, the index dropped 6.06, or
0.9%.
The Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600 Index advanced 2.68, or 0.77%, to 350.77 on Friday. For the week, the
S&P 600 lost 2.21, or 0.6%.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 14.20, or 0.66%, to 2160.35 on Friday. For the week, the Nasdaq dropped
15.16, or 0.7%.
Friday's volume on the Nasdaq was 2.34 billion shares. Up volume exceeded down by 1.38 billion shares to
931 million, and rising stocks beat decliners, 1,906 to 1,135.

Gold Prices Hit a 17-Year High As Demand for Jewelry Sparkles
Commodities Report
Gold Prices Hit a 17-Year High As Demand for Jewelry Sparkles
By Allen Sykora Dow Jones Newswires
449 words
17 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold futures on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange rallied yesterday to 17-year highs,
supported by the voracious demand for the metal and technical chart-based buying.
September gold rose $4 an ounce to $459.50, and the most-active December contract gained $4 an ounce to
$463.30 after peaking at $464.
"The market has broken out technically," said Bill O'Neill, one of the principals with Logic Advisors.
"Perhaps the most impressive element of the gold rally is that it is not doing it in sync with the dollar," said
Mr. O'Neill. "It is doing it independently, which is a very positive sign."
Metals often move inversely to the dollar. For the week, however, December gold added $10.30, while the
New York Board of Trade's dollar index rose from a close of 86.89 last week to a high yesterday of 88.30.
"Gold is moving on its own fundamentals," said Paul McLeod, vice president for precious metals at
Commerzbank.
He and Mr. O'Neill pointed to the strong jewelry demand. The consultancy GFMS this week reported that
jewelry fabrication for gold in India was up 50% during the first half of the year, compared with a year ago.
"The physical supply/demand equation of the market is really quite constructive," said Mr. O'Neill. "Interest in
gold as an alternative asset has been growing. It's really becoming an almost mainstream asset. If you had
said that three or four years ago, people would have looked at you like you were crazy."
Spot gold hit its record high of $852 back in 1980, Mr. McLeod said. That figure would be much higher in
inflation-adjusted terms. In 1980, Comex gold hit a high of $873, independent technical analyst Jim Wyckoff
said.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures on the New York Mercantile Exchange fell almost $2 on the growing perception that oil
demand has taken a hit from the high prices caused by Hurricane Katrina and that released reserves of oil
from around the world have covered the shortfall caused by U.S. production shut-ins. The October contract
fell $1.75 to settle at $63 a barrel.
GASOLINE: Nymex futures plummeted 6% to the lowest price since Aug. 4 as traders sold off positions in the
belief that demand is going to be weak because of a typical autumn fall-off in driving and that a huge amount
of imports attracted by high prices will swell supply. October gasoline futures fell 11.36 cents to $1.7851 a
gallon.

points, or 0.50%, to 10544.90, off 2.2% this year.
Reflecting the worry about oil's impact on inflation and economic growth, gold finished at its highest level
since Dec. 7, $449.80 an ounce, up $3.70 on the day.
"The market had a tremendous move up" after Hurricane Katrina hit, said Todd Leone, head of listed trading
at brokerage firm SG Cowen. "I just think we were a little toppy. As oil started moving up, the market started
coming off. People are still worried about interest rates a bit, and whether the Federal Reserve is going to
keep going" with its campaign of interest-rate increases.
The Fed meets Tuesday to decide whether to push its target short-term lending rate up by another
quarter-point to 3.75%. Such a move was a near certainty until Katrina hit.
Soft reports on August retail sales and industrial production also hurt stocks, suggesting a weakened
pre-Katrina economy. Lehman Brothers announced strong quarterly results, which initially helped financial
stocks, but even those sagged as oil rose. News that Delta Air Lines and Northwest Airlines were close to
filing for bankruptcy protection also weighed on the market. (Both airlines did, after the close.)
Oil futures rose $1.98 to $65.09 a barrel, a 49% 12-month gain. Gasoline futures rose for a second day to
$1.9374 a gallon, up 60% in 12 months. The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.33%, or 4.04 points, to
1227.16 and the Nasdaq Composite Index declined 1.03%, or 22.42 points, to 2149.33.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks and bond prices retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.49 billion shares traded, 1,207 stocks rose and
2,050 fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 8/32, or $2.50 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield up to
4.162%. The 30-year bond was down 18/32 to yield 4.450%. The dollar eased.

Bayou Transfers Set Off Alarms --- As Money Moved About the Globe, The Stories Got More Creative; Bank Employees Blow the Whistle
Bayou Transfers Set Off Alarms --- As Money Moved About the Globe, The Stories Got More Creative;
Bank Employees Blow the Whistle
By Ian McDonald, John R. Emshwiller and Ianthe Jeanne Dugan
2,579 words
12 September 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IT LOOKED LIKE a high-stakes game of three-card monte.
In the months before Bayou Management LLC's hedge funds collapsed, its founder sent most if not all of
what was left of his investors' money ricocheting around the world -- from New York to London, Germany,
Hong Kong and Flemington, N.J. Sam Israel III also signed an investment contract that was supposed to
alchemize the bulk of the money -- $100 million -- into $7.1 billion.
The game ended, authorities say, only after Mr. Israel turned the $100 million over to a New Jersey man who
tried to continue shuffling the money around, aided by a Kentuckian, a pair of Europeans and a small-time
Hollywood moviemaker. Sensing something suspicious, employees of several financial institutions helped
Arizona authorities seize the $100 million so it can be returned to Bayou's investors.
A detailed look at these machinations, culled from court records and interviews, offers an inside glimpse of
what authorities say is a little-known netherworld of investment charlatans. It also shows how antifraud
tripwires now arrayed throughout the U.S. finance system have increased banks' vigilance.
Prompted initially by criminals hiding ill-gotten gains and more recently by terrorist financiers, state and
federal authorities have pressured financial institutions to be ever more wary of accepting large unexplained
deposits. Bank executives long have complained that money-laundering rules are onerous and costly.
In the Bayou case, it was branch-level employees who blew the whistle. "Everyone in the banking industry
has gotten religion," says Ian Comisky, a money-laundering expert. Banks report lots of innocuous activity to
authorities "defensively," he adds, "but it seems like they picked up something correctly in this case."
Authorities say Bayou investors' money was being used in a convoluted and timeworn scam usually
attempted on a much smaller scale. The scheme involves promising investors eye-popping returns to get a
hold of their money. Then the con artists persuade a series of banks to accept the cash by overwhelming
low-level employees with financial gobbledygook -- just plausible enough to pass muster but ultimately so
nonsensical that the banks look negligent later. The grifters then make off with the money, leaving victims
with only an impenetrable money trail to follow and flatfooted bankers to sue.
Investigators say Mr. Israel was either the conmen's co-conspirator or a swindler who got swindled -- a
hapless mark making a last-ditch $100 million bet to recoup losses after wildly exaggerating results for years.
He didn't respond to numerous messages.
Bayou's woes burst into public view last month, after Mr. Israel, 46 years old, announced he was closing shop
but failed to return investors' money. Authorities say investors had entrusted his Stamford, Conn., firm with
more than $300 million, and Bayou claimed that those assets had grown to well over $400 million. But federal
prosecutors say in a Sept. 1 civil suit that the firm had "overstated gains, understated losses and reported
gains where there were losses" since 1998, the year after it started investing. The suit calls Bayou's assets,
including the $100 million seized by Arizona, "the proceeds of criminal activity" and demands that all
remaining funds to be turned over to the federal government for return to the investors.
Prosecutors say Bayou began trying to cover its tracks in April 2004 with a dizzying series of transactions.
First, it transferred $150 million from Citibank in New York to a securities account at Barclay's Bank in London
before returning the money to Citibank. Then, in July 2004, Bayou emptied its five Citibank accounts of $161
million. About $60 million of that remains unaccounted for, including $32 million wired somewhere in the U.S.
On July 8, 2004, $120 million was wired from the Citibank accounts to a German bank, Bayou lawyers say in
court papers, and then $115 million of that was transferred to another German bank on Oct. 25, 2004.
On Dec. 5, 2004, Mr. Israel and Bayou Chief Financial Officer Daniel Marino, meeting as Bayou's board,
authorized Mr. Israel to sign a $100 million investment contract for money to be held at a London securities

--- Page 46 ---
Page 46 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
firm under his and Bayou's name. The next day, about $121 million from the second German bank -- the gain
came from euro's value increasing -- was wired to the London securities firm, but in Mr. Israel's name only,
Bayou says in court papers.
THEN THINGS WENT from odd to truly bizarre, according to Arizona court filings.
On March 29, 2005, Bayou hired Southern California businessman Lewis P. Malouf as "director of
investments." Little is known about Mr. Malouf, but local federal court records show a person with the same
name and a similar signature filed for bankruptcy protection in 2002 to avoid eviction.
That same day, Messrs. Israel and Malouf, 61, signed a 12-page contract, all in capital letters, under which
Mr. Malouf would oversee two "private, managed, buy/sell leveraged transactions" totaling $100 million.
Without explaining how, the document projected returns of $7.1 billion over 10 years.
To execute the investment, Mr. Malouf turned to Karl Johnson, who runs a company called Majestic Capital
Management from his home in Flemington, N.J., says Paul Robbins, a lawyer for Mr. Johnson. The lawyer
says Mr. Johnson, 64, never met Mr. Israel. Mr. Robbins says his client "is not involved -- nor did he have the
intention of being involved-with anything illegal."
Mr. Malouf declines to discuss the matter in detail. "All of this information is inaccurate," he says via email. He
specifically denies any business dealings with Mr. Johnson or Majestic.
Back in April 2004, just as Bayou started moving funds around the world, Mr. Johnson went to Wachovia
Bank's Flemington branch and opened the first of several accounts for Majestic, Arizona authorities say in
court papers. Bank employees told investigators that Mr. Johnson regularly called about big pending deposits
from overseas that never materialized.
This time was different: In mid-April 2005, $99 million was transferred from London to a Wachovia branch in
Hong Kong, before being sent to one of Majestic's Wachovia accounts, records show. Another $809,000 or
so was wired there a week or two later. (Bayou lawyers say in court papers that the money came directly from
accounts controlled by Mr. Israel, including the London one that previously had held $121 million, but filings
by Arizona officials say the funds came through other accounts.)
Mr. Johnson told a Wachovia banker that the money belonged to a wealthy New Yorker he declined to
identify, lest Wachovia poach his business, and would be invested in "bond deals" by Dietmar Hormann, a
German money manager for an outfit named Advanced Asset Management, authorities say. (Mr. Hormann
says Mr. Johnson hired his firm, based on a reference from a mutual friend in California, to help invest $100
million.)
Messrs. Johnson and Hormann wanted to move the money from the bank account to a Wachovia Securities
brokerage account. Mr. Hormann contacted Douglas Falconer, a Hollywood producer with some small movies
to his credit for whom Mr. Hormann was trying to raise capital. On April 25, Mr. Falconer made a "cold call" to
a Wachovia Securities branch in Los Angeles, where he was connected to broker John Carswell. (Mr.
Falconer confirms calling Mr. Carswell as a favor to Mr. Hormann, but denies knowingly taking part in any
scam.)
Mr. Falconer told Mr. Carswell that Mr. Hormann and a colleague, Nebile Brougham, would contact him.
When they did, Mr. Carswell was told that they wanted Majestic's $100 million moved to a brokerage account
via a "MT103 transfer," Arizona officials say in court papers.
Mr. Carswell was baffled: Why would a Hollywood filmmaker call him on behalf of a German trader? Why
move the money from New Jersey? And what was an MT103 transfer? Mr. Carswell had no idea, nor did his
coworkers. His assistant, Nilsa Santiago Sabir, asked Mr. Johnson, but his explanation was "confusing and
not making any sense," Arizona authorities say in court papers.
Ms. Santiago referred the matter to Wachovia Securities' antifraud unit, where compliance analyst Austin
Jones did an Internet search that turned up a mention of Mr. Hormann and Ms. Brougham in connection with
"an alleged $300 million fraud" in Hong Kong weeks earlier, court papers say. Mr. Jones emailed the find to
Ms. Santiago. "It shows beyond a shadow of a doubt that this is a scam," he wrote.
Mr. Hormann and Ms. Brougham, who is based in Geneva, deny involvement in fraud. He says his firm
bowed out of the Hong Kong deal when questions arose about it. They also say they stopped dealing with Mr.
Johnson. They say they knew nothing of Bayou or its deal with Mr. Malouf and thought Mr. Johnson owned
the $100 million. "Why should anyone give him funds? Maybe an idiot from Timbuktu," says Ms. Brougham.
Back in Flemington, Mr. Johnson was now a regular at the Wachovia branch. On April 29, he asked to
transfer the $100 million to his brokerage account. Banker Clarinda Estevez told him her authority was limited

--- Page 47 ---
Page 47 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
to $500,000 transfers and, in any case, the brokerage account hadn't been opened. He became upset, saying
he was in danger of losing $15 million and his "position." Ms. Estevez asked what he did for a living.
"Young lady, you shouldn't be asking those questions," he replied, Arizona authorities say in court papers.
NOW THE FLEMINGTON bankers were suspicious, too: Except for Majestic's initial Wachovia account, all
the others -- including the $100 million one -- had been opened over the phone, and there was "no normal
business activity" on any of them, authorities say. The circumstances reminded Ms. Estevez of a fraud she'd
observed at another bank.
Mr. Johnson returned later that Friday and persuaded another employee to open a brokerage account and
transfer the $100 million. The transaction was rejected because that employee also had insufficient authority.
Unaware of that, Mr. Johnson sent flowers the following Monday. The card said something like, "Thank you
ladies for all your work," court records say.
Ms. Estevez reported the matter to Wachovia Bank's antifraud unit the next day, May 3, three weeks after the
money arrived. Within days, Wachovia told Mr. Johnson to withdraw the $100 million by June 16.
Mr. Johnson then turned up at a Smith Barney brokerage and asked to set up a $100 million account. There,
Arizona authorities say his story changed: He said Majestic Capital Management was a unit of the Majestic
Foundation, an offshore charity, and profits from the account would finance AIDS clinics around the world. He
said the money was "good, clean and cleared funds." The brokerage rejected Mr. Johnson's business and
referred the matter to Smith Barney's antifraud unit.
Next, Mr. Johnson went to a Commerce Bank branch, which accepted the $100 million deposit on about May
10. But after an employee drove by Majestic's business address and found it to be Mr. Johnson's "modest
residence," the matter was turned over to a bank compliance officer, Don Temple.
Mr. Temple called Mr. Johnson and received yet another story: The money had been held in London for three
years and belonged to the Maloof family -- no relation to Mr. Malouf -- owners of the Sacramento Kings
basketball team. He mentioned the two Europeans and said they would be buying "Fed-rated" bonds at
discounts and selling them for certain profit on the "Euro clear screen." He refused to explain further, saying
he feared the bank would steal the idea from him.
Mr. Johnson then had a Kentucky associate, Tedd Collins, call Mr. Temple, Arizona authorities say. Mr.
Collins related yet another story, authorities say: The money was from a loan secured by an Arizona gold
mine worth $152 billion, authorities say. (Mr. Collins didn't return calls.)
A day or so later, the Arizona's attorney general opened its investigation. Authorities there won't say who
tipped them off or why the tipster called authorities in a Southwestern state. But the matter ended up with one
of the nation's most prominent money-laundering cops -- Cameron "Kip" Holmes, an assistant attorney
general who helped draft a 1985 money laundering statute for Arizona that became a model for other states.
Wachovia, Commerce and Smith Barney were subpoenaed on May 13.
Within days, Commerce Bank returned the money to Wachovia. Mr. Holmes's investigators quickly concluded
that much of what Mr. Johnson and his associates had said was false. When the Maloofs' money manager
was asked if the family had $100 million in a bank account, "he laughed out loud," court papers say. The story
about the $152 billion Arizona gold mine story also was implausible: Less than $8 billion in gold had been
mined in the state since it joined the union. As for Mr. Collins, Wachovia determined that an account he
maintained at another bank in Kentucky had been used in an attempted $22 million counterfeit-check fraud,
court papers say.
The authorities decided they had stumbled upon a massive attempt at what experts call "prime bank
instrument fraud." Perpetrators, Mr. Holmes explains, "use specific language, like MT103, that implies some
kind of special knowledge, but it's just an emperor's-new-clothes situation." The funds are funneled through
various institutions to "remain ahead of the trailing train of original victim investors," an Arizona investigator
adds in court papers.
When victims come looking for their money, each fraudster along the trail claims to be a victim of the previous
one. To deflect blame, the Arizona investigator says, they trick "generally inexperienced front-line employees
of large financial institutions" into accepting their money, while purposefully leaving behind evidence of fraud
-- exposing the bank to civil suits, sometimes filed by the fraudsters themselves.
That's what Arizona officials say was attempted at Wachovia, Smith Barney and Commerce Bank. "These
three financial institutions are really good examples of compliance people working smoothly with customer
service people," says Mr. Holmes. "It's also an excellent example of people resisting the lure of a big deposit."
(Wachovia and Smith Barney declined to comment; representatives of Commerce Bank didn't return calls.)

--- Page 48 ---
Page 48 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
On May 19, an Arizona state court seized the money, finding evidence that it was "being used in a fraud on
various financial institutions," the state says. Arizona wants to hand the matter over to federal authorities. The
federal probe continues.
In early June, Bayou sent its clients an update on their investments. "The final two weeks of the trading month
of May were full of hope and promise," it said. The firm's bets had "paid off well."
---
Cast of Characters
Some of the players involved in Bayou's complex financial trail:
-- Sam Israel III: Bayou's founder and chief executive
-- Lewis P. Malouf: Southern California businessman; signed $100 million
investment contract with Bayou
-- Karl Johnson: Flemington, N.J., money manager; ended up in control of
Bayou's $100 million
-- Tedd Collins: Kentucky associate of Mr. Johnson
-- Dietmar Hormann: German money manager; hired to help invest the $100

-- Nebile Brougham: Geneva-based associate of Mr. Hormann
-- Douglas Falconer: Small-time Hollywood filmmaker; tried to help Mr.
