Prices Are Higher, but So Is the Dollar In a Twist That Fans Fears of Inflation
Commodities Report
Prices Are Higher, but So Is the Dollar In a Twist That Fans Fears of Inflation
By Peter A. McKay
840 words
1 August 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THIS YEAR'S CHAPTER in the long-running commodity rally features a real plot twist: Prices are up despite
the absence of a weakening dollar, one of the usual suspects in the rise in everything from crude oil to frozen
orange juice.
Analysts say this turn of events confirms that people really want to take delivery of the commodities
represented by futures contracts that trade on exchanges in Chicago and New York. Commodity bears have
long grumbled that -- since most commodities are bought and sold globally in dollar terms -- it takes more
greenbacks to buy the same amount of resources whenever the dollar weakens. Their beef: Commodity
rallies in such an environment aren't as impressive as they seem at first glance.
But with prices of everything from corn to palladium higher for the year, even as the dollar remains stronger
than last year's levels against the euro and the yen, even the bears are conceding the commodity sector's
strength isn't just a figment of big-looking prices expressed in terms of a debased currency.
If both commodities and the dollar finish the year higher, it would be the first simultaneous annual rise since
2000, which was marked by the slowing of a years-long economic expansion.
Now the new grouse: If the new pattern continues, it could rekindle fears of commodity-led inflation, as
importers and manufacturers snatch up raw materials to produce the finished goods demanded by expanding
economies in China, the U.S. and elsewhere.
"Supply is constrained right now for all the commodities, but especially energy," says strategist Francisco
Blanch, of Merrill Lynch. "At the same time, demand looks solid for at least the next six to 12 months."
The Reuters Jefferies CRB Index, a broad-based commodity indicator, is up 11% this year to 315.24, and the
Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index is up 9%. The J.P. Morgan Dollar Index, a trade-weighted measure of the
dollar's value against a basket of other currencies, has risen 4%. In each of the past three years, commodities
have climbed and the dollar has weakened.
To be sure, there have been exceptions to the latest commodity uptrend, and the CRB index remains off its
annual high of more than 320. Livestock, coffee and cocoa all are down on the year. Gold, which is arguably
the most dollar-sensitive resource, has slipped 1.9%, or $8.50, to $429.90 a troy ounce in New York futures
trading. Because of its traditional role as a tangible, widely accepted means of financial exchange, the metal
often rallies when investors lose confidence in government-issued currency. Conversely, it falls when the
paper money strengthens, as it has this year.
Other key commodities have dodged the pitfall of a stronger dollar so far, starting with crude oil. Futures have
leapt 39%, or $17.12, to $60.57 a barrel, near a nominal record on the New York Mercantile Exchange,
pushed higher by continuing tightness in the balance between global demand and refining capacity of about
80 million barrels a day.
Other energy products, including gasoline and natural gas, have followed crude sharply higher. Copper
futures -- an important measure of industrial activity -- are up 13%, or 19.2 cents, to $1.6445 a pound on
Nymex's Comex division.
Looking ahead through the end of the year, the major question for both commodity and currency traders will
be how the economies of the U.S. and China are affected by the latter's recent decision to revalue the yuan.
In recent years, China has emerged alongside America as a leading consumer of natural resources, so any
shift in the balance of trade between the two could have big implications for the commodity markets. A
stronger yuan will tend to make Chinese exports to the U.S. more expensive here, thus less competitive. It
also will make dollar-denominated imports -- such as commodities -- less expensive in China, thus somewhat
easier to buy and consume.

--- Page 142 ---
Page 142 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Economist James Glassman, of J.P. Morgan Chase &Co., says he expects commodities prices to ease in the
second half of the year, but he also is skeptical that China will allow the yuan to rise too strongly against the
dollar, because the country is so reliant on exporting finished goods.
"Their economy has really just left the runway for takeoff," Mr. Glassman says. "They still need to worry about
creating manufacturing jobs for the people in their small towns, away from the major cities."

LIVE CATTLE: Futures prices at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange rose on technical chart-related buying and
the covering of previously established sold, or short, positions on the last trading day of the month. Higher
prices fetched in cash cattle trade late in the week also supported the futures market. Live cattle for August
delivery rose 1.62 cents to 81.20 cents a pound.

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...and Not: Markets' Faith Grows in U.S. Rally
--- Indexes Abroad Also Gain As Decliners Include Gold; Shares Leap 10% in Seoul
By Peter A. McKay
617 words
1 August 2005
The Wall Street Journal

C3
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Analysts say the overall trend suggests that markets are gaining confidence that an economic slowdown in
the U.S. isn't imminent -- even if it is still a concern further down the line, perhaps beginning in the fourth
quarter.
"I think people have been caught a little off guard by the strength we've seen lately," says Joe Williams,
director of equity strategy at Commerce Trust Co. "The longer the economy hangs in there, the more we'll
see corporate-earnings surprises to the upside, which will help stocks."
One major overseas beneficiary of the U.S. market's resurgence was the Seoul Composite Index, which leapt
10%, or 103.13 points, to 1111.29. The Bombay Sensex 30, French CAC 40 Index and German DAX 30
Index all rose more than 5% in July.
Despite daily declines Friday, the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index and the Nasdaq Composite Index
reached four-year highs late in the month, while the Dow Jones Industrial Average hasn't done so yet. The
technology-heavy Nasdaq registered the biggest monthly increase among them, gaining 6.2%, or 127.87
points, to finish at 2184.83.
"I'm encouraged that technology is up there among the best performers," says Michael J. Cuggino, president
and chief executive of the Permanent Portfolio Family of Funds. "I think some pockets are already fairly
valued, like the chip makers. But there are other areas, like software, that I think are barely starting to come
out of the doldrums."
On the heels of a pair of refinery fires in the Southwest, crude oil prices jumped 7.2%, or $4.07, to $60.57 a
barrel, finishing the month near the $61.28 nominal record reached July 6 on the New York Mercantile
Exchange.
The continued spate of expensive crude prices has helped Exxon Mobil Corp., which last week announced a

component of the Dow industrials, gained $1.28 on the month, or 2.2%, to finish at $58.75 on the New York
Stock Exchange.
Giant oil producers aside, energy prices have been a bugaboo for most of the stock market for months. But
analysts said any harm from last month's rise in crude oil was offset by a lessening of jitters over surging U.S.
housing prices.
Analysts say investors world-wide are becoming comfortable with the idea of high home prices as a measure

about housing and the Fed's monetary policy. The central-bank chief encouraged changes in the regulation
of major housing lenders Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, but he avoided dire descriptions of a looming

"Everyone believes Alan Greenspan when he tells us the economy is slowly but surely recovering," Mr. Jonas
says. "A few months ago, people were more worried about hitting a soft spot."
Gold futures, which are often considered a hedge against economic weakness, dropped 1.6% in July, or
$7.20, to $429.90 a troy ounce on the New York Mercantile Exchange's Comex division.
The Dow Jones U.S. Basic Resources Index, which includes companies that produce a variety of
commodities, rose 21.72 points, or 9.6%, to 204.75 on the month.

Art &Money: A Market Dries Up --- Islamic Art Prices Fall After Arrest Of Collector; $1.57 Million Fly Whisk
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Art &Money: A Market Dries Up --- Islamic Art Prices Fall After Arrest Of Collector; $1.57 Million Fly
Whisk
By Marcus Baram
1,345 words
29 July 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IN THE CLOSE-KNIT world of Islamic art, prices are dropping and master works are going unsold. Many
dealers and auction-house experts attribute the market's decline to a single factor -- the absence of collector
Sheik Saud al-Thani.
Mr. al-Thani, a 38-year-old member of the royal family of Qatar, was arrested there in March. The
government's audit bureau is investigating allegations that he misappropriated state funds, buying art for
himself with money designated for Qatari museum purchases. He has since been released, but a government
spokesman wouldn't comment on any charges or his legal status.
After his arrest, April sales at Christie's and Sotheby's in London -- the most important auctions in Islamic art
-- plunged 70% from a year earlier. At Sotheby's, just over one-third of lots sold, compared with almost
two-thirds in year-earlier auctions. In those 2004 auctions, Mr. al-Thani spent an estimated $26.4 million on
Islamic art, a category that includes furnishings, jewelry and religious objects from across the Muslim world.
Among his purchases: an agate-and-garnet Mughal fly whisk from India he bought at Christie's for GBP
901,250 ($1.57 million at current exchange rates) -- more than 100 times its top estimate of GBP 8,000. He is
widely credited with helping prices in the sector nearly triple from 2001 to 2004. Mr. al-Thani couldn't be
reached for comment.
At this year's London auctions, however, many of the priciest items failed to sell, or sold at the low end of their
estimates. A 17th-century Mughal sword with a high estimate of about $140,000 failed to sell, while a marble
water jar from 10th- to 12th-century Egypt sold for $250,000, below its high estimate of about $316,000. At
the market's lower end, many pieces are relatively inexpensive: Only 26 out of 242 items at Christie's April
auction sold for more than GBP 10,000 ($17,600).
"The Islamic art market was artificially inflated and now it's deflated -- and most of that's due to Sheik
al-Thani," says Linda Komaroff, the curator of Islamic art at the Los Angeles County Museum of Art. Ms.
Komaroff says Mr. al-Thani drove up prices by spending heavily on "sometimes-mediocre" art. "Now more
collectors -- collectors with smaller budgets -- have a chance to get into the market," she says.
Over the past eight years, Mr. al-Thani spent millions in his capacity as head of Qatar's National Council on
Culture, Arts and Heritage. He was assigned to purchase artworks to stock the five Islamic art museums
scheduled to be built in the tiny desert country. Mr. al-Thani did not often appear personally at auctions, and
was known for aggressively outbidding other collectors. Sometimes, auction-house officials say, he would
have two representatives bidding on his behalf on the same object to scare off competitors.
His purchases extended well beyond Islamic art, as he amassed a collection that included everything from
contemporary photography to vintage cars. Acquisitions included a $9.57 million Faberge egg, a complete set
of Audubon bird prints for $8.8 million and a Roman marble nude. In all, dealers estimate, he spent $1 billion
over the last 10 years on artwork.
Qatar's cultural council is conducting an inventory of Mr. al-Thani's personal collection to determine which
pieces he bought with government money, an official at the country's audit bureau says. Qatar's prosecutor
general's office declined to comment.
The Islamic art market is dominated by a few collectors, including Kuwaiti royal Sheik Nasser Sabah Ahmed
al-Sabah, the Sultan of Brunei and Nasser David Khalili, an Iranian-born property investor based in London.
Some collectors, including Mr. al-Sabah, stopped buying much Islamic art in the mid-1990s. "I know quite a
few collectors who withdrew. They would get depressed at constantly being the under-bidder," says Edward
Gibbs, the director of Islamic art at Sotheby's.

--- Page 146 ---
Page 146 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. al-Thani is not the first collector to have a big impact on a sector of the art market. In the 1990s, British
advertising executive Charles Saatchi helped create a market for contemporary British artists such as Tracy
Emin and Damien Hirst. Tom Monahan, chief executive of Domino's Pizza, boosted prices for architect Frank
Lloyd Wright's windows and furniture in the '90s, a market that still hasn't recovered following his pullout.
Some say the impact of Mr. al-Thani's withdrawal is limited, however. As with Old Masters and Impressionist
paintings, the supply of high-quality Islamic art is shrinking, and that could bump up prices. To declare a
market-wide decline based on April's sales results "is like saying that the entire Impressionist market has
slumped because some of the paintings in a large ordinary Impressionist auction failed to sell," says Mr.
Khalili, whose 20,000-piece collection, which includes calligraphy and Quranic manuscripts, is said to be the
world's largest private collection of Islamic art.
Still, the recent decline in sales is unusual for a market that tends to be rather stable, according to most
dealers. Unlike their colleagues in Western art markets, Islamic dealers tend to avoid the high-risk
speculation that sends prices soaring and plummeting. Price fluctuations occurred in the 1970s, when Iranian
oil wealth drove up prices of Qajar pieces (art and design created during a 19th-century dynasty that ruled
parts of Iran). In the 1980s, a strengthening Turkish economy helped spark a surge in prices for Iznik
ceramics, from a pottery-making center in Turkey. But until the surge from 2001 to 2004, prices for Islamic art
tended to actually be lower than 19th-century prices for the same pieces, when adjusted for inflation.
Some observers of the Islamic art market believe the Qatar government still needs to buy works for its
museums, the first of which is set to open in 2006. Paulson Edutan, Mr. al-Thani's former executive secretary
at the country's cultural council, says he'll be missed. "He was the one person in Qatar who knows
everything" about what art to buy, Mr. Edutan says.

Currencies -- Changes &Chatter: Chinese Government Staffers Get Quick Lessons in Volatility
Currencies -- Changes &Chatter: Chinese Government Staffers Get Quick Lessons in Volatility
By James T. Areddy
717 words
26 July 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SHANGHAI -- Here inside a former Russian bank building on this city's famed waterfront Bund, the impact of
China's currency revaluation hits home.
Built in 1905 as the Far East headquarters for St. Petersburg Russo-Asiatic Bank, the building now houses
the China Foreign Exchange Trade System, the country's currency market. At one time, the brick and
concrete structure was notable as the only place in Shanghai with indoor plumbing. Last week, the 2.1%
revaluation in the yuan nearly overnight made the market inside a key facet of China's financial system.
Because the yuan's value had virtually never changed since the market opened in 1994, Wang Qinhuai, vice
general manager of research at the center, was able to devote some afternoons in recent years to playing
ping-pong on a table just outside his fourth-floor office.
This year, the ping-pong table was removed. Now the yuan is set to begin bouncing. China's central bank
pledged the currency "will be more flexible," a fact bankers and policymakers say should be regarded as a
more fundamental change than the higher exchange-rate level. When China pushed up the value of its
currency last week, it also said it was adopting a managed floating exchange rate: The yuan can rise or fall as
much as 0.3% a day against the U.S. dollar depending on supply and demand.
In the two sessions so far under the new system, trading has stuck to a tight range in Shanghai, but the yuan
is moving. The U.S. dollar ended a bit lower yesterday at 8.1097 yuan after popping slightly higher on Friday
following the revaluation to 8.11 yuan. Activity was $1.1 billion yesterday after the busier-than-normal $2.13
billion on Friday.
On Thursday, few staffers at the exchange knew yuan adjustment was imminent before the revaluation was
announced that night on state television. A half-hour after the broadcast, said one, his phone rang at home:
Head back to work as soon as possible. Exchange officials worked until 4 a.m. adjusting and testing the
electronic platform Chinese banks use to buy and sell yuan.
Missing on that cloudless, full-moon night were members of the open-market-transactions department. They
are the central bank's traders and had rushed to Beijing for quick lessons on how to actually execute
flexibility in the yuan's exchange rate. In particular, said the exchange official, they got a primer in the
secretive new "basket" formula that the central bank says it now refers to. Instead of valuing the yuan solely
against the U.S. dollar, the central bank said it would introduce -- though it didn't reveal details of -- a way to
gauge the yuan's appropriate value against other currencies.
Stationed on the second floor of the Shanghai trading center, just up the building's Y-shaped marble
staircase, the central bank's traders effectively manage China's exchange rate in the market. Their buying of
U.S. dollars at specified levels from banks that want to sell them has kept the yuan steady in recent years.
Selling yuan for the U.S. currency is the technical process that has stuffed China's central bank with $700
billion in foreign-exchange reserves.
As anticipation of a yuan revaluation grew in the past year, and on the hope that currency "volatility" might
emerge, the world's biggest banks began elbowing for market share in yuan trading. More than 70 foreign
banks now operate in China, and more and more of them are members of the Shanghai trading system. The
center, which also offers bond and gold trading, has a trading floor, but most banks connect through a
computer system.
For bank treasury departments, a moving currency equals potential for profit. Bankers expect that, as the
yuan is gradually permitted to rise and fall more freely, banks' corporate customers will demand more
sophisticated financial products. Ultimately, how much business there is will depend on how quickly Chinese
officials liberalize currency-exchange rules, not only on how much they let the yuan's value adjust to
international supply and demand pressures.

Currency Decision Marks Small Shift Toward Flexibility --- Move Follows Broader Embrace Of `Floating Rate' Systems By Developing Countries...
Currency Decision Marks Small Shift Toward Flexibility --- Move Follows Broader Embrace Of
`Floating Rate' Systems By Developing Countries --- Using Singapore As A Model
By Jon E. Hilsenrath and Mary Kissel
1,540 words
22 July 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
China took only a small step away from a rigidly fixed currency system yesterday. But its actions still marked
a big step in the 30-year evolution of the global financial system toward more market-driven, flexible
exchange rates.
Countries generally choose either a "flexible" policy for their currencies, allowing them to trade freely in often
ruthless global financial markets, or a "fixed" approach, keeping the currency stable but losing control of other
economic levers like interest rates.
China, mindful that painful tradeoffs come with either approach, is trying to chart a middle path. Using tiny
Singapore as its model, it aims to allow some market flexibility while maintaining many government controls.
In addition to moving the yuan up a bit, it said it would end the yuan's link to the dollar and instead let it trade
in a narrow range tied to a "basket" of currencies -- with details of the currencies and their mix kept secret.
It also promised "greater flexibility," a suggestion that the target the government sets for the currency will be
determined, at least in part, "based on market supply and demand."
The move ultimately could set China on a course traveled by many developing countries in recent years.
Some 139 countries, most of them small, moved from fixed to flexible exchange rates between 1990 and
2002, according to the International Monetary Fund.
But it's been a painful ride for many of them, a history that is not lost on Chinese officials. Well over half of
those countries made the switch under what the IMF delicately calls "disorderly" circumstances -- meaning
financial or economic crises.
Argentina, for instance, which had long tied its currency to the dollar, abandoned the link in 2002 because the
high interest rates then needed to sustain the currency's value were strangling the economy and banks. In
the 1990s, other countries -- such as Mexico and Thailand -- were forced to abandon their dollar links when
investors fled their markets and their banking systems, deprived of capital, melted down. Both countries were
thrown into deep recessions.
The choice between a fixed-rate system and a flexible-rate system can be unappetizing, since both carry
risks. Defending a fixed exchange rate -- under which a currency is set at a predetermined rate -- can force
policy makers to push up interest rates to preserve their currency's value and possibly wreck their
economies. A flexible exchange rate -- under which a currency's value "floats" against other currencies -- can
absorb shocks, but also exposes a country's financial system to speculative attacks and sudden devaluations
that can hurt an economy.
Some economists say a middle ground could carry even greater risks, because it opens a country's currency
to market forces but leaves officials without all the tools to defend it. China, however, clearly is betting that it
is in a position of strength in charting its own course, because of its huge financial resources and, importantly,
because of government controls on the movement of capital in and out of the country.
"The world's largest developing economy has clearly gone down the route of the middle," said Jeffrey
Frankel, a Harvard University international economist who worked in the Clinton White House. "This is not a
country that is moving in the direction of a firm fixed [exchange rate] and it is not a country that is moving in
the direction of a firm floating."
The global currency system has evolved significantly since a system of fixed-exchange rates among major
economies was negotiated after World War II. The original goal of the victorious powers was to create a new
world economic order that, they hoped, would avoid the chaos that followed the collapse of the gold standard
in World War I and exchange-rate turmoil that contributed to the Great Depression. The system, known as
Bretton Woods for the New Hampshire resort where it was negotiated, called for the U.S. and Europe to

--- Page 151 ---
Page 151 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
maintain fixed exchange rates, with some flexibility to make adjustments in times of economic crisis, under
the watchful eye of the International Monetary Fund.
The Bretton Woods system ran into trouble in the 1960s, in part because U.S. trade deficits mounted and the
nation lacked financial reserves to support the currency. It finally came unmoored in 1973, and most major
currencies began floating against their counterparts. A number of Europe's major economies surrendered the
ability to adjust their currencies against each other with the creation of the transnational euro in 1999. But the
euro moves freely in financial markets against the dollar.
Developing countries were slower to make the move. Still, bound to the developed world by trade but often
too small to navigate the global financial system safely, many have chosen or been forced to do so. In 1975,
87% of developing countries had some type of pegged exchange rate, according to the IMF. By 1996, the
figure was below 50%, and it has continued to fall. Chile, Israel and Poland moved to flexible regimes
gradually, while Turkey and Brazil made such moves more abruptly. Hong Kong stands out as an exception:
Despite China's move, the Hong Kong dollar remains fixed to the greenback.
"Exchange-rate flexibility is extremely important as the economy keeps opening up," says Ramkishen Rajan,
a visiting economics professor at the Lee Kuan Yew School of Public Policy in Singapore. A country with a
fixed exchange rate essentially must buy its currency when foreign investors try to sell large amounts, in
order to sustain its value. That can drain its foreign-currency reserves -- and push interest rates higher.
China's exchange rate has stayed stable, despite the gobs of capital pouring into the country, only because
its central bank has bought billions of U.S. dollar assets, such as U.S. Treasury debt. Chinese technocrats
and economic officials around the world saw its approach as unsustainable, in part because buying dollars for
yuan made it difficult for China's central bank to manage the supply of credit internally.
China also has avoided another common problem for countries with fixed exchange rates. They usually run
into trouble when economic and market conditions argue for a lower value and they can't do what's needed to
prop up the currency.
China is in the opposite situation, with a currency that appears undervalued. That's a more comfortable
problem to tolerate, but comes with its own share of possible pitfalls, inflation being one of them. China's
controls on capital have been important in limiting, though not eliminating, the ability of citizens and
speculators to put pressure on its currency, making it easier for the government to manage the currency than
it is for countries where capital flows more freely.
Singapore adopted its basket approach in 1981, when memories of oil shortages, stagflation, and exchange
rate volatility of the late 1970s remained fresh. It was seen as a halfway house between fixed and flexible
currencies.
The advantage of a basket is that a currency isn't pulled way out of whack by sudden market moves, for
instance when the dollar moves sharply against the euro, as has been the case recently.
It is known as a "managed float," meaning that the government doesn't let the markets set its value without
limit, but doesn't try to maintain a fixed peg either. Especially in a smaller economy, a fixed peg can be
broken when speculators bet billions against it, as several Asian countries learned in 1997. China has
technically operated a managed float since 1994, but is now expected to allow more flexibility.
Singapore's approach, closely studied by the Chinese in recent months, has been an economic success: Per
capita income has risen substantially, inflation has been contained and interest rates have stayed low,
encouraging consumers to spend. Despite essentially open borders for goods and capital, authorities have
managed to keep the Singapore dollar relatively stable, tolerating a slowly rising trend.
Singapore doesn't reveal exactly how its basket of currencies is constructed, an approach China appears to
be emulating. Because they don't know which currencies are in the basket, market participants are kept
off-guard, and are reticent to make big bets against the central bank. Slowly, the central bank can edge the
exchange rate up or down -- a technique called a "crawl" -- rather than do it in one, big jolt.
While the model has worked well for Singapore, it's less clear that it can work for an economy as large as
China's. Singapore's gross domestic product is only half as much as Wal-Mart's annual revenue.
Moreover, because of its smaller size and the government's firm grip on power, Singapore's central bankers
have a number of ways besides interest-rate moves to steer the economy that a larger country like China
may not. For example, by tinkering with rules on how Singaporeans can use funds in their mandatory
pensions accounts, the Monetary Authority of Singapore has in the past influenced prices in the housing
market.

Will There Be Another Wizard?
Will There Be Another Wizard?
By Marc Sumerlin
1,337 words
20 July 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In 1900, Frank Baum published "The Wonderful Wizard of Oz," a literary endorsement of a move to a
bimetallic dollar based on both gold and silver. Fueled by William Jennings Bryan's speeches, the dispute
over the nation's money supply had reached its rhetorical highpoint well before the Federal Reserve was
even created.
Bryan and the populist pro-silver movement lost the debate and the elections of 1896 and 1900, despite their
better command of language. But their goal of reflation happened anyway. Vast new deposits of gold were
subsequently discovered in South Africa, Alaska, and Colorado, increasing the money supply enough to allow
prices to rise over 40% between 1897 and 1914. Politically, the debate was really about the preferred rate of
money-creation and whether to favor in-debt farmers with easy money or the powerful city financiers with tight
money. But the more fundamental choice was between a discretionary monetary policy in which politicians
decided how much money was created and a discretion-free system enforced by gold.
A century later, an actual wizard of monetary policy is making perhaps his last appearance before
Congress, and the president must find a replacement. The monetary debate has evolved, of course, since
1900, with nearly everyone now agreeing that inflation should be kept as close to zero as possible without
letting prices fall. But there is a great debate over how best to accomplish this goal, and whether other goals
like risk management and full employment deserve a seat at the Fed's weighty table.
Once again, the central choice is between monetary policy driven by human judgment or by a set of rules
that constrain discretion. The current vogue in central banking is the movement toward inflation targeting,
whereby the central bank uses an explicit inflation target as the primary guide to making monetary policy.
While contemplating what monetary policy will look like in the next era, the president and Congress should
also consider whether it would be a safer course to have a more rule-based policy. Inflation targeting has
been tested recently in places like the U.K., Brazil and Mexico, and is most credible when there is some
accountability of the central bank to the target. Inflation targeting makes great sense in places without a
long history of effective independent monetary policy and can help anchor long-term interest rates.
Whether it makes sense for the U.S. is a more difficult question. In the semi-annual Monetary Policy Report
to the Congress, the Fed today will announce their expected range of inflation based on the presumption of
appropriate monetary policy, which last February was between 1.5% and 1.75% for 2005 and 2006. With
long-run interest rates near historic lows and inflation expectations exceedingly well contained, there is little
to be gained at the moment from making this target more binding. Most importantly, the Fed may want to
keep this powerful tool in reserve in case it ever loses its hard-won credibility.
More worrisome, inflation-targeting could actually erode the credibility of the central bank during familiar
circumstances. Suppose an oil-price shock temporarily accelerated inflation above the formal target. Even if
the central bank expected economic growth to falter and future inflation to subside, it may worry that an
interest-rate cut would erode its credibility even more than the initial breach of the inflation target. This isn't a
hypothetical situation, but one in which the European Central Bank has found itself this year.
While the crowning achievement of the Greenspan Fed has been the achievement of price stability, it has
accomplished even more. It has reacted promptly to domestic financial crises by lending through the discount
window immediately after the October 1987 stock market crash and by pumping in over $80 billion in liquidity
just after Sept. 11, 2001. The Fed also cut interest rates by about 80 basis points in the four months
following the stock market crash and by 175 basis points in the three months after Sept. 11. In each case, the
Fed's actions were critical to maintaining confidence in the U.S. economy at a vulnerable time. This
crisis-management role is not simply a side-show. Responding to financial crises as a lender of last resort
was one of the primary reasons for establishing the Federal Reserve, which was created in response to the
particularly severe banking panic of 1907. For better or worse, the Fed is also as close as there is to an
international central bank, having more power and standing than the IMF.

--- Page 156 ---
Page 156 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In 1998, the Fed responded indirectly to the Russian debt default, which spread into the U.S. via the LTCM
hedge fund, by lowering interest rates 75 basis points over four months. Responses to international events
are much more controversial, and there are legitimate arguments to criticize, in hindsight, the Fed's action,
given how strong the U.S. economy was at the time. But in the era of globalization and leverage, the Fed
needs to preserve the option of acting. A restrictive inflation-targeting regime would risk cutting off this
alternative without even knowing what risks will turn into events over the next decade. A close look at China's
banking system is enough to make any seasoned economist know that systemic risk is not a quaint problem
of the last century.
A concrete inflation-targeting regime also raises the delicate question of what constitutes inflation. There
are serious technical questions with existing measures, such as the different treatments of housing and
medical care by different indices and the lack of chain-weighting by the headline CPI. Even more difficult is
the question of how the Fed should handle rapidly rising asset prices, like the NASDAQ's 86% climb in 1999
or the housing market's double-digit-and-still-accelerating pace of today.
While the Fed doesn't have the ability to know what level asset prices should be at, it must have a general
view on whether it is creating too much liquidity. And liquidity can raise assets prices just as it can raise
wages or goods prices. Along the same lines, the Fed must have a view on whether leverage is excessive,
especially since it has control of margin requirements and has the ability to weigh in on all areas of finance.
Another key consideration to the inflation targeting debate is whether the Fed should also be concerned
about full employment. Its independence is a gift of Congress, not an inalienable right. The 1978 Full
Employment and Balanced Growth Act amended the Fed's charter to make the bank's mandate the pursuit of
both stable prices and maximum employment. Inflation targeting puts one goal explicitly ahead of the other,
and a change to a rigid system would politically require the consent of Congress.
The narrow debate on inflation-targeting is part of a larger debate on how critical decisions are made. Are
decisions ultimately best left to human judgment, allowing the human brain to tap into its immeasurable
supply of theoretical, historical and real-time anecdotal evidence? Or is it better to allow a model to precisely
calculate an answer based on quantifiable assumptions and potentially stale data? I'll bet if you gave
investors or business leaders a choice of having access to the Fed's macroeconomic model or Alan
Greenspan's frontal lobe, almost all would choose the lobe.
There is a split among replacement candidates between those who favor a more rule-based monetary policy
and those who favor a completely discretionary policy. Mr. Greenspan himself favors a discretionary,
risk-based policy. As a first step toward making a decision on the next Fed chairman, the president and
Congress may want to decide whether they are looking for another wizard or someone who follows the rules.
---
Mr. Sumerlin, former deputy director of the National Economic Council under President Bush, is managing
director of the Lindsey Group.

Oil Prices Fall Amid Hurricane Cleanup in Gulf
Commodities Report
Oil Prices Fall Amid Hurricane Cleanup in Gulf
By Leah McGrath Goodman Dow Jones Newswires
453 words
15 July 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Crude-oil futures in New York shed more than $2 on the barrel, settling at a two-week low amid rising oil
inventories and a sharp recovery in oil and natural-gas production along the U.S. Gulf Coast.
Benchmark light, sweet crude futures for August settled down $2.21, or 3.7%, at $57.80, marking the lowest
close since June 30. The selloff, which also fed off signs this week that global oil-demand growth may slow,
pulled gasoline futures to their lowest level in more than a week and drove heating oil futures to a two-week
low.
The U.S. Minerals Management Service reported yesterday that production halted in the Gulf of Mexico after
last week's hurricane fell to just 3.6% of daily oil output and 2.7% of daily natural-gas output -- a strong
rebound from double-digit percentages on Wednesday.
Traders' belief that global oil demand may be cooling stemmed from a report earlier this week indicating
Chinese oil-demand growth was slowing, according to a Chinese state-run news agency. A report
Wednesday from the International Energy Agency appeared to confirm that, with the Paris energy watchdog
revising lower its oil-demand growth forecasts for the year.
"One thing I am starting to see is more articles on lower oil demand, especially in China," said Conrad Goerl,
a partner and trader at MotherRock LP, a New York energy hedge fund. "That story seems to be getting a
little bit more traction."
Indications that Hurricane Emily may head away from the main U.S. refining region of the Gulf also gave
traders a reason to sell. After two weeks of storms that interrupted Gulf supply, forecasts predicting Emily
may head elsewhere assuaged supply fears, traders said. Others warned that Emily could disrupt oil supply
from countries such as Venezuela and Mexico, top suppliers to the U.S. Mexican oil facilities, in particular,
may end up a target, as Emily could barrel into the Bay of Campeche, home to Mexico's largest oil field, said
R. Lewis Ropp, an energy analyst for Barrow, Hanley, Mewhinney &Strauss, a Houston institutional fund
manager.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Prices on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange fell, pressured by a rising dollar
and commodity-fund selling. August gold slipped $4.30, or 1%, to $419.60 an ounce.
LUMBER: Prices at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange closed at the exchange-imposed daily price limit as
traders bought back previously sold positions when cash trade firmed. Most-active September lumber rose
$10, or 3%, to $334.70 a 1,000 board feet.

`Hot Money' Helps Drive Oil Volatility
Going Global
`Hot Money' Helps Drive Oil Volatility
By Michael R. Sesit and David Reilly
1,066 words
14 July 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
London -- FOR INVESTORS in the oil market, the ride keeps getting wilder.
When news of terror attacks in London broke last Thursday, "black gold" plunged about 7%, then rebounded
6% -- all within a few hours. Such massive one-day swings are rare, even in typically volatile commodity
markets. But oil investors might have to get used to them.
With the price of oil having doubled over the past two years to about $60 a barrel, the market is on a knife's
edge. Because of tight supply and gargantuan demand, $100-a-barrel oil may not be out of the question,
some analysts say. Yet if, as some others believe, energy demand is peaking, particularly in China, the price
could just as easily tumble to $30 a barrel. Into this fundamental disagreement steps more and more
so-called hot money chasing a fast profit.
Of course, natural disasters or man-made shocks, such as severe storms or political revolutions, have always
caused oil prices to fluctuate. On Tuesday, after news emerged that BP's Gulf of Mexico Thunder Horse
offshore platform -- the biggest in the world -- was found severely damaged after Hurricane Dennis -- the
price of oil in New York soared 4% to $61.25. It later gave back some of the gains to close at $60.62, a 3%
rise from the previous day's close. Yesterday on the New York Mercantile Exchange, crude oil for August
delivery settled at $60.01 a barrel, down 61 cents.
What has changed is the market's ability to deal with short-term crises. Until a few years ago, a sharp price
shift would be tempered by the view that oil ultimately would have to move back into line with long-term price
assumptions. These consensus views, which help people calculate the price they are willing to pay today for
oil contracts coming due anywhere from five to 10 years down the line, were based more on oil-industry
economics than short-term supply-and-demand factors.
Analysts generally agreed the long-term price of petroleum, largely derived from the likely cost to produce a
barrel of oil, was $20 to $25 a barrel. In October 2000, when the USS Cole was attacked, the spot price for oil
for immediate delivery rose to about $38 a barrel from about $35, but the price of oil for delivery in five or 10
years hovered around $20. The gap reflected the view that at some point, prices would fall closer to long-term
production costs.
Now, longer-dated contracts have risen with spot prices. Yesterday, five-year contracts were trading at about
$57 while the spot price was at about $60. This reflects uncertainty about the appropriate level for future
prices based on production costs and on where future supply will come from, said Jeffrey Currie, head of
commodities research at Goldman Sachs in London.
Analysts and investors are perplexed by new and unpredictable factors in the energy system: the thinnest
margin of spare oil-pumping capacity in decades, the rapidly growing oil demand of China, rising taxes in
developing countries that will boost the cost of producing oil and the growing role of speculators and other
financial investors in the oil markets.
That is translating into more-extreme market moves. In the wake of the Sept. 11, 2001 attacks, oil fell about
25% on fears of a slump in travel, tourism, consumer confidence and global economic growth. That drop took
nine days to play out.
On July 7, after the London bombings, the front-month August contract for crude oil on the New York
Mercantile Exchange fell to $57.20 from $62.10 in about 90 minutes. The $4.90 price swing was the biggest
move in dollar terms since Jan. 9, 1991, although it occurred during after-hours trading in the U.S., so volume
was thin, and that potentially exacerbated the move.
After investors surmised the London attacks wouldn't have a far-reaching economic impact, prices bounced
back as quickly as they had fallen, closing at $60.73.

--- Page 164 ---
Page 164 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The oil market has gathered such speed thanks, in part, to the increasing number of pure speculators who,
unlike airlines, utilities and oil companies, have no direct need to buy petroleum. Many in this so-called
hot-money crowd are hedge funds that use computer programs to game trends in financial markets. When
these trading programs spot a big price shift, they use futures markets to latch onto the momentum, often
causing a move to gain an even greater pace.
Describing last Thursday's trading as "really insane," Don Casturo, a senior energy trader at Goldman Sachs,
said, "There's more hot money, and that definitely was what was behind [Thursday's] move." Oil, he added,
"is certainly more of a toy than it was in the past."
The growing clout of speculative players in the market exacerbates the uncertainty caused by potentially
too-little future supply contrasted with the currently huge, but unpredictable, energy appetite of China and
India. Noting oil-company forecasts that world demand for oil will rise 50% in the coming 20 to 25 years, Mark
Mathias, chief executive at Dawnay Day Quantum, a specialist fund-management unit of Dawnay Day Group,
said: "There is not enough oil from currently known reserves to meet this level of demand for any length of
time."
Production costs are rising, too, especially because many oil-producing countries in the developing world --
where oil companies now must turn for new supply -- are increasing taxes, said Goldman's Mr. Currie. The
unpredictability of these tax plans undermines investors' ability to form a consensus on long-term prices, he
added.
Such uncertainty hasn't necessarily hurt investors willing to bet on oil's rise. Through July 12, gasoline prices
have soared 57% this year, heating oil has rallied 46% and U.S. light, sweet crude oil is up 44%, according to
Deutsche Bank.
Increased volatility may bode well for investors who invest directly in oil rather than in, say, oil-company
stocks. "I'd argue the one safe haven is going to be in the commodity itself," Mr. Currie said. "Unlike equities,
where volatility is bad, with commodities you're more likely to be rewarded for it."
---
Send questions and comments to michael.sesit@wsj.com.

Small-Stock Focus
Urologix Rises, Majesco Declines On Lower Forecast

brings June retail sales results and monthly readings of the consumer-price and producer-price indexes.
The impact of these inflationary measures is important, said Michael Cuggino, manager of the Permanent
Portfolio Family of Funds. That's because if the CPI climbs, as businesses pass more of their cost increases

China Data Ease Pressure Over Yuan --- Weaker Inflation Undercuts U.S. Calls for Revaluation; Concerns About Deflation
International
China Data Ease Pressure Over Yuan --- Weaker Inflation Undercuts U.S. Calls for Revaluation;
Concerns About Deflation
By James T. Areddy
821 words
13 July 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SHANGHAI -- Tame inflation in China is undercutting arguments by U.S. Federal Reserve Chairman Alan
Greenspan and other prominent economists that Beijing must revalue its currency to avoid economic
overheating.
With China expected to announce official economic figures for the first half by the end of this month,
economists are predicting that the numbers will show a continued weakening in price pressures and subdued
money-supply growth. Chinese policymakers have resorted to an unconventional mix of market and
administrative tools to cool the economy. That could take some of the urgency out of any official plans by
Beijing to revamp the country's rigid currency system and exacerbate already-prickly trade ties between
China and the U.S.
The U.S. and China's other major trading partners have called on China to revalue the yuan, which is pegged
to the U.S. dollar. They say the Chinese currency is undervalued by as much as 40%, making the country's
exports unfairly cheap and fueling huge trade surpluses with its trading partners. China so far has resisted
such calls; last month, Premier Wen Jiabao said Beijing would determine any changes to its currency system
"in accordance with China's needs."
China's economy remains a powerhouse. It continues to absorb massive amounts of foreign investment and
its June trade surplus grew faster than expected. Beijing says the economy remains on track to grow 9% this
year.
But price pressures are easing. China's National Bureau of Statistics yesterday said consumer prices are
expected to rise 2.2% in 2005, well below last year's 3.9% and earlier government predictions of a 2005
inflation rate of 4%. Foreign economists predicted that consumer prices would rise 1.6% in June over the
previous year -- which would be the slowest rate this year -- according to a recent poll by Dow Jones
Newswires.
Indeed, some economists are voicing concern that China could return to a period of deflation, where prices
fall, as took place from 1998 to 2003.
Some economists urge Beijing to encourage personal consumption, which they say could help to support
prices and satisfy foreign companies hoping to sell more products in China. "I don't think changing the
exchange-rate regime is an urgent thing," said Wang Xiaolu, an economist at the National Economic
Research Institute in Beijing.
Andy Xie, chief regional economist for Morgan Stanley in Hong Kong, points to lagging imports as an early
sign that overcapacity in heavily invested manufacturing and property sectors has caught up with China, with
raw materials like oil in less demand. Concerns of inflation are misplaced, he said, adding: "Greenspan does
not appreciate the nature of an investment-led economy."
Mr. Greenspan was among the earliest proponents arguing for a yuan revaluation. Since June 2003, he has
repeatedly said China should -- and likely, would -- adjust the yuan upward to protect its own financial
system. Last month, Mr. Greenspan batted down calls from U.S. lawmakers to level trade sanctions against
China if it doesn't let the yuan rise, saying China's rigid exchange-rate system carries risks of "triggering
upward pressure on inflation and a general overheating of the Chinese economy."
Chinese policymakers have long acknowledged that they need to revamp the exchange-rate system, and
they may still decide that a revaluation of the yuan in the near future is in the country's interest. Even a small
upward adjustment of the yuan could help Beijing signal its willingness to address its bulging trade surplus

--- Page 171 ---
Page 171 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
with the U.S. and other countries. Some economists anticipate just such a goodwill gesture before President
Bush's expected visit to China this fall.
So far, however, China seems to be successfully reining in excessive growth by using a mix of
market-oriented methods, like an interest-rate increase, and administrative efforts, including clampdowns on
property lending, new taxes and outright price controls. Beijing has said market measures alone aren't
effective because of China's poorly developed financial system.
Some economists argue that inflation pressures aren't well reflected in official numbers. "Inflation
expectation at this point is not fully contained," said Hong Liang, an economist at Goldman Sachs in Hong
Kong. She said bubbly property markets and rising consumer demand for gold reflect efforts to hedge
against inflation.
China's central bank has stamped out heady money-supply growth by absorbing bank-system cash in a
technical process that some see as extremely risky in the long term. This process of sterilization has grown
especially quickly this year. Stephen Green, an economist at Standard Chartered Bank in Shanghai, said in a
report published yesterday that more foreign money "than ever before" from trade, investment and hot money
flows could ultimately mean inflation later.
---
Jason Dean in Beijing contributed to this article.

Stocks Stand Up to Terror --- Last Week's Quick Rebound Reflects the Dow's Tendency To Shrug Off Most Attacks
Abreast of the Market
Stocks Stand Up to Terror --- Last Week's Quick Rebound Reflects the Dow's Tendency To Shrug Off
Most Attacks
By E.S. Browning
1,529 words
11 July 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS LIVE with concern that a terrorist attack will tank the stock market. But a look at some of the
past century's biggest terrorist attacks shows that has rarely happened.
The trend could be seen in the surprisingly rapid U.S. stock recovery after the terrorist attacks in London last
Thursday. More broadly, an analysis of market reaction following 15 different terrorist acts going back to 1920
suggests that, while terrorism may be a generalized cause of fear, it has rarely been a major mover of
markets.
Terror fears unquestionably have made investors more conservative, helping to explain the surprising
popularity of investments such as bonds, real estate and gold. Investors react most strongly to events that
they think will have a lasting impact on markets or the economy, such as the Sept. 11, 2001, attacks and they
have learned to shrug off others.
"The attacks in London were horrific, to be sure, but their market impact is likely to be short-lived because the
events are not likely to have a sustained impact on economic conditions, monetary policies or capital flows,
anywhere," wrote Bob Prince of money-management firm Bridgewater Associates in a report to clients.
The data indicate that, even immediately following a terrorist event, the Dow Jones Industrial Average has
tended to rise.
On the first day that stocks traded following each of the 15 attacks, the Dow industrials finished up eight
times, including last Thursday, and down seven. By the end of the fifth trading day after the other 14 attacks,
it also was more likely to be up. After about a year, the Dow was down in only four instances, and the
average change was a gain of more than 13%, according to the analysis, conducted for The Wall Street
Journal by Ned Davis Research in Venice, Fla.
The 15 events studied began with the 1920 anarchist bombing at the J.P. Morgan building on Wall Street,
which killed 40. They also included an attack on Congress in 1954, as well as hostage-takings, hijackings and
bombings in the Middle East, Europe, Asia, Africa and Oklahoma City.
Among these events, the biggest one-day market decline came after trading reopened following the 9/11
attacks, when blue chips fell 7%. The next-biggest event-day decline followed the bombing of a TWA flight in
1974, a 2.2% plunge. After that was the Madrid train bombing in March 2004, a 1.6% one-day drop. Other
event-day declines were minor. Over the one-week and one-year periods, the changes were random enough
that most appeared more attributable to normal market forces than to terrorism.
The 9/11 attack was especially damaging because investors feared lasting economic impact and worried that
another attack might follow. Some analysts, however, argue that investors got over that shock faster than it
seemed. In this view, later market declines were due mainly to a downward stock-market spiral already under
way after the popping of the technology-market bubble. Whichever is true, terrorist events since haven't
seemed to have had the same disruptive impact.
"We find evidence that suggests that modern U.S. capital markets are more resilient than they were in the
past and that they recover sooner from terrorist/military attacks than other global capital markets," wrote
Andrew Chen of the Cox School of Business at Southern Methodist University and Thomas Siems, senior
economist at the Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas, in a study published last year.
One way to see this is to compare the market impact of terrorist events with that of war. Stocks took more
than three years to recover from the German invasion of France in 1940. They took the better part of a year
to recover from Pearl Harbor. The Vietnam War, and the inflation it helped to spur, weighed on markets
throughout the 1970s.

--- Page 173 ---
Page 173 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Last week, it took U.S. investors less than a day to get over the London bombings. To the amazement of
analysts, stocks actually finished higher on Thursday, the day of the attack, and gold finished lower. By
Friday, European markets, too, had largely recouped their losses.
The week's big financial news turned out to be Friday's employment report, which suggested a "Goldilocks"
combination of low wage inflation and steady job creation, which investors welcome. On Friday, the day after
the London bombings, the Dow Jones Industrial Average jumped 146.85 points, or 1.4%, to 10449.14, the
biggest one-day gain since April. It was up 145.70 points, or 1.4%, for the week, but remains down 3% for
2005.
What seems likely is that terrorism's more profound impact on financial markets comes from the broader fear
it creates, which has contributed to a cooling of enthusiasm for stocks. Partly because of the tech-stock
bubble's popping, and partly because of terrorism fears, many investors have turned to real estate, bonds,
gold and other commodities, deeming them safer than stocks. That may help explain why demand for
Treasury bonds has remained surprisingly strong in recent years, holding the yield of the 10-year Treasury
note down near 4%, far lower than almost any analyst had predicted.
---
Friday's Market Activity
Alcoa rose $1.11, or 4.3%, to $27.20 after the aluminum maker reported better-than-expected second-quarter
earnings.
Charles Schwab fell 18 cents, or 1.4%, to 12.50 after gaining ground for several days on speculation the
company could be taken over. The company on Thursday said it plans to stay independent.
General Electric climbed 81 cents, or 2.4%, to 34.99 after analysts at Smith Barney and Morgan Stanley
made positive comments about the stock's valuation.
Accenture added 2.01, or 9.1%, to 24.21. The company's fiscal third-quarter earnings rose 45%, thanks to
growth in outsourcing and consulting. The company provided a fourth-quarter forecast in line with Wall
Street's expectations and raised its full-year guidance.
XL Capital dropped 2.89, or 3.9%, to 70.99. The insurance provider said it will record a second-quarter
charge of $183 million to strengthen reserves for its North American reinsurance operations. Merrill Lynch cut
its rating on the stock to "neutral" from "buy," saying the charge may "negatively impact management
credibility."
Shaw Group fell 1.07, or 4.7%, to 21.55. The Baton Rouge, La., company swung to a loss during the fiscal
third quarter because of charges related to the retirement of debt and the valuation of a deferred-tax asset.

digital set-tops, modems and other equipment is better than it has been for several years.

Markets Shudder, Rebound Quickly --- Industrials End Up 31.61 Points As European Stocks Trim Losses; Analysts See a New Resiliency
Thursday's Markets
Markets Shudder, Rebound Quickly --- Industrials End Up 31.61 Points As European Stocks Trim
Losses; Analysts See a New Resiliency
By E.S. Browning and Mark Whitehouse in New York and David Reilly in London
1,066 words
8 July 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
FINANCIAL MARKETS SEEM to be getting more resilient in the face of terrorist attacks.
After shuddering in the wake of London's bombings, world financial markets rebounded strongly as trading
wore on. The Dow Jones Industrial Average, down more than 220 points in futures trading before regular U.S.
trading began, finished up 31.61 points, or 0.31%, at 10302.29, still down 4.5% for 2005.
European markets trimmed heavy early retreats to finish with less-severe declines. Down nearly 4% at one
point, London's FTSE 100 share index finished at 5158.30, down 1.4%.
Safe-haven investments, such as U.S. Treasury bonds and gold, jumped on initial reports of the bombing,
but then gave back most or all of their gains; gold finished down on the day. By day's end, investors were
turning their attention back to the Labor Department's much-awaited June employment report, due today,
which will give an indication of U.S. economic strength as well as the likely impact of wage increases on
inflation.
Asian stock markets edged higher as trading resumed in the region on Friday.
Terrorism continues to make investors cringe and to weigh on their optimism. But money managers sought to
reassure clients that, terrible as the London events were, experience showed that they weren't likely to have a
lasting impact on financial markets. Analysts produced data showing that, following the bombings in Madrid
last a year, financial markets recovered within days.
"While today's events have brought real pain and suffering to those directly impacted -- and a renewed fear to
the rest of us -- they are not unprecedented," Tim Swanson, chief investment officer of the private-client
group at Cleveland's National City Corp., said in a message to clients as regular U.S. trading began. "If we
look to history as a guide, the initial selloff will likely reverse course as the initial shock gradually fades from
view."
His advice: "Resist the temptation to follow the fearful crowd headed for the exits."
Investor concerns now focus less on the loss of human life and more on whether terrorism will cause a lasting
disruption to society. The overriding fear in the investment community, often unspoken, is something on the
level of a biological attack or a dirty bomb -- a bomb laced with radioactive materials that would render
sections of a big city uninhabitable -- or a large-scale attack like Sept. 11, 2001. When confronted with
smaller-scale attacks, investors tend to panic initially and then gradually begin going about their business
again.
"There is a striking difference between the Sept. 11 attacks and today, because Sept. 11 was totally
unexpected," said Paola Subacchi, head of the international economics program at Chatham House, a
London-based research concern. "People in Britain are used to terrorist attacks because of the [Irish
Republican Army's] activities going back decades. Everyone knew London could have been a target."
The European Central Bank and the Bank of England both held regularly scheduled meetings on interest
rates. Both left their benchmark interest rates unchanged, at 4.75% in Britain and 2% for the ECB. Federal
Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan spoke by telephone with European Central Bank President Jean-Claude
Trichet and Bank of England head Mervyn King. They determined that financial markets were functioning
properly and that there was no need to pump money into the banking system, as occurred after the Sept. 11
attacks.
Oil also fell on news of the violence, on fears that the bombings could further slow world growth. Oil had been
up sharply in a belief that economic growth could push supplies to the limit. Likewise, the bombings hurt

--- Page 178 ---
Page 178 of 224 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
stocks of economically sensitive companies, such as makers of steel, chemicals and business equipment.
Hotel, insurance and transportation stocks also were hit. By day's end, however, fears for the economy were
dissipating and industrial, insurance and travel-related stocks were bouncing back, some finishing with gains.
Crude-oil futures finished down just 55 cents at $60.73 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange, below
Wednesday's 22-year high but still above $60.
The dollar went through the same drill -- down heavily at first, but recouping most of its gains by day's end.
The British pound, however, finished the day with sharp losses.
After being up more than 1% during the day, gold finished down 20 cents at $423.30 an ounce.
Heavy early buying of 10-year U.S. Treasury notes pushed the notes' price up and their yield as low as
3.94%, from about 4.07% Wednesday. But by day's end, the 10-year yield was back above 4%. European
government bonds acted similarly.
"Maybe the market's getting somewhat better at digesting this," said Bill Kohli, a bond-fund manager at
Putnam Investments in Boston.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index yesterday rose 0.25%, or 2.93 points, to 1197.87, down 1.2% this
year. The Nasdaq Composite Index gained 0.34%, or 7.01 points, to 2075.66, down 4.6% for 2005.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 1.05%, or 1.78 points, to 167.92.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.51 billion shares traded, 1,787 stocks rose and 1,449 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 6/32, or $1.875 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
down to 4.051%. The 30-year bond was up 10/32 to yield 4.309%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 112.08 yen, down from 112.18 yen, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.1943 from $1.1928.
---

Stocks Succumb to Oil's Surge --- Dow Industrials Decline 101.12, Lowest Finish Since May 16; Crude Jumps Past $61 a Barrel
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Succumb to Oil's Surge --- Dow Industrials Decline 101.12, Lowest Finish Since May 16; Crude
Jumps Past $61 a Barrel
By E.S. Browning
514 words
7 July 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCK INVESTORS were willing to overlook oil's rebound until the price crossed $60 a barrel again, at
which point stocks took a hit.
With Gulf of Mexico storms disrupting production, oil futures jumped $1.69 to $61.28 a barrel on the New
York Mercantile Exchange. It was the highest closing level since oil futures started trading on the Nymex in
March 1983, and the highest close on an inflation-adjusted basis since August 1983. Crude oil prices have
risen 41% this year.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 101.12 points, or 0.97%, to 10270.68, its lowest finish since May 16. It
now is down 4.8% for 2005.
"The market couldn't overcome oil," said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at brokerage firm SG Cowen.
"You could really see it develop during the day. As oil started moving up, the stock market just went down."
Futures on unleaded gasoline and heating oil also surged to their highest price levels in more than 20 years.
Investors fear high oil prices will slow economic growth. In addition, the stock market often pulls back when
the Southeast is threatened with serious storms, Mr. Leone added, because no one knows how bad the
damage will be for the regional economy, insurance firms and the energy sector.
Making things worse, U.S. layoffs hit a 17-month high last month, according to outplacement firm Challenger,
Gray &Christmas. That raised concerns about the June employment report due from the Labor Department
tomorrow, which investors watch closely as an indicator both of economic strength and of wages' impact on
inflation.
The unsettling economic news pushed bonds higher. A slower economy tends to hold down interest rates,
making existing bonds more attractive.
Investors did get one bit of good news -- that the U.S. service economy, representing more than half of all
economic activity, expanded faster than expected in June, according to a report from the Institute for Supply
Management. But the oil news outweighed that report.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.83%, or 10.05 points, to 1194.94, down 1.4% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index declined 0.49%, or 10.10 points, to 2068.65, down 4.9% for 2005.
The dollar hit its highest level against the yen in almost a year in late New York trading, although it fell against
the euro. Gold rose after hitting a one-month low on Tuesday.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks fell and bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 10/32, or $3.125 for each $1,000 invested,
pushing the yield down to 4.074%. The 30-year bond was up 22/32 to yield 4.327%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, the currency traded at 112.18 yen, up from 111.59, while the euro
rose against the dollar to $1.1928 from $1.1917.

Dollar Holds Steady; Euro Bounces Back From Low
Currency Trading
Dollar Holds Steady; Euro Bounces Back From Low
By Laurence Norman Dow Jones Newswires
596 words
6 July 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar fell slightly versus the euro but remained steady against other major currencies as it took a pause
after several days of gains.
Overnight, the euro fell to a fresh 13-month low at $1.1868 and the dollar hit a 48-week high versus the yen at
112.13 yen.
However, stronger-than-expected European data and profit-taking meant the U.S. currency was unable to
hold those gains. During New York trading, the euro bounced around $1.19, while the dollar stayed below
112 yen. U.S. financial markets were shut Monday for the July 4 holiday, and there was no market-moving
U.S. data yesterday.
Late in New York, the euro was at $1.1917, up from $1.1896 late Monday in North American trading. The
dollar was at 111.59 yen from 111.52 yen and at 1.3034 Swiss francs from 1.3033 francs, after tapping an
almost 14-month high of $1.3073 overnight.
The pound was at $1.7571, lifting from the 13-month low it tapped overnight at $1.7506 and from $1.7576 a
day earlier. The euro was at 132.98 yen from 132.83 yen.
Traders said the dollar gains also ebbed because of market caution ahead of the meeting of heads of state
and government of the Group of Eight leading nations this week in Scotland and U.S. nonfarm payrolls data
Friday.
The euro was also helped by the calming of speculation that the European Central Bank might announce a
rate cut after its meeting tomorrow.
Monday's U.S. holiday didn't stop the dollar extending the latest round of gains that began late last week
following a slew of strong U.S. data and another rate hike from the Federal Reserve.
Expanding interest-rate differentials between the U.S. and the euro zone, Switzerland and Japan have helped
the dollar stage a sharp recovery in 2005 -- particularly over the past few months.
Andy Busch, global market strategist at Harris Nesbitt in Chicago, said he expects further dollar gains in
coming days.
"This has still got some oomph behind it," Mr. Busch said, adding that with the yen under pressure from a
fresh rise in oil prices back to around $60 per barrel, the U.S. currency could spring over 112 yen again.
Still, others were less sanguine about the dollar's fate going into the G-8 session and the payrolls number.
"I think it's going to be rather quiet" in the days ahead, said John McCarthy, director of foreign exchange at
ING Barings Capital in New York. "My guess is that we'll see some consolidation for now."
Meanwhile, the euro got a boost after a report showed euro-zone retail sales up a surprisingly high 1.1% in
May, following April's revised 1.1% decline. Year-over-year sales were up 2% versus April's 0.8% decline.
Elsewhere, the Australian dollar was hit hard, with rising U.S. yields and a sharp fall in gold and other
precious metals prices pushing the currency to an eight-month low.
The Australian dollar also fell against the euro and the yen -- a sign that growing risk aversion may be
dimming the carry trade, where investors borrow in low-yield currencies to buy assets of higher yielding ones.
The currency was at 74.25 U.S. cents from 74.97 U.S. cents a day earlier.
